Divine Attributes
beginner
5 min
Divine Attributes: Neither Identical Nor Separate
Al-Bitar’s marginal note explaining attributes are neither identical nor separate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari position that Allah's attributes are neither identical to His essence nor separate from it, and the rational proofs for this.

Core Idea
Allah's attributes are neither the same as His essence nor something separate—like the number one in ten.

This lesson examines a key clarifying note by Sheikh al-Bitar on a phrase from the 'Aqidah al-Tahawiyyah: the attributes of Allah are 'neither the essence itself nor other than it.' This may sound paradoxical, but it is a precise theological formulation that guards against two extremes: identifying attributes with essence (redundancy) and separating them from essence (implying deficiency). Through logical proofs and a helpful analogy, the passage establishes the middle path of Ash'ari theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Pay attention to the verse from Bad’ al-Amalī and the analogy.
2
Understand the two negations
Notice that the phrase 'لا عين ولا غير' is explained in two parts: 'not identical' (obvious) and 'not other' in the technical sense of inseparable.
The technical meaning of 'other' here is crucial.
3
Grasp the proofs and analogy
Follow the two proofs: if identical, redundancy; if separate, deficiency. Then see how the analogy of one and ten resolves the paradox.
The analogy shows conceptual difference without existential separation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
علق الشيخ محمد البيطار في الحاشية قائلا: «قوله التي هي لا عين ذاته ولا غير ذاته. أما كونها لا عين فظاهر؛ لأن الصفة ليست عين الموصوف، وأما كونها لا غير أي بالمعنى الاصطلاحي وهو ما لا يمكن انفصاله عن الذات وصفاته تعالى لا تنفك عن ذاته أزلا وأبدًا. قال صاحب بده الأمالي رحمه الله تعالى : من رساری تعا عن
صِفات الله لَيْسَتْ عَيْنَ ذَاتٍ ولا خَيْرًا سِوَاهُ ذَا انْفِصَالِ
قال الشارح: لأنه لو كان عين ذاته يلزم الترادف بين اسم الذات ووصفه وهو محال، وأما الثاني فلان الغيرين هما اللذان يمكن انفصال أحدهما عن الآخر؛ فلو كانت غير ذاته لاتصف غير ذاته بها وهو محال؛ لأنه يلزم أن توجد صفاته الكاملة في غيره فيكون ناقصا في ذاته مستكملا بغيره وهو باطل.
وقوله: "ذا انفصال" إشارة إلى تفسير الغير؛ أي: المراد من غير الشيء ما ينفصل عنه بحسب الوجود لا ما يغايره بحسب المفهوم؛ لأن ما يفهم من الذات غير ما يفهم من الصفات بالإجماع؛ فأحدهما غير الآخر بالضرورة، وإن كانت غير منفصلة عن ذاته في الوجود؛ فثبت أن صفات الله تعالى لا هو ولا غيره؛ كالواحد من العشرة؛ فإنه ليس عين العشرة ولا منفصلا عنها. انتهى منه الشيخ محمد البيطار
Sheikh Muhammad al-Bitar commented in the margin saying: 'His statement: “which are neither the essence itself nor other than the essence.” As for it not being the essence itself, it is obvious because the attribute is not the essence itself. As for it not being other, i.e., in the technical sense, which is that which cannot be separated from the essence, and His attributes are never separate from His essence, eternally and forever. The author of Bad’ al-Amalī, may Allah have mercy on him, said: “From his treatise…? The attributes of Allah are not the essence itself, nor other than it that is separate.” The commentator said: Because if it were identical to the essence, there would be redundancy between the name of the essence and its description, which is impossible. As for the second [possibility], because two things that are other are those that can be separated from each other; if they were other than the essence, then other than the essence would be described by them, which is impossible, because it would entail that His perfect attributes exist in another, so He would be deficient in His essence and perfecting Himself through another, which is false. And his statement: “that is separate” refers to the interpretation of ‘other’: i.e., what is meant by ‘other than something’ is that which separates from it in existence, not that which differs from it in conception. Because what is understood of the essence is different from what is understood of the attributes by consensus; so one is necessarily other than the other, even if they are not separate from the essence in existence. Thus it is established that the attributes of Allah are neither Him nor other than Him, like the one of ten: it is neither the ten itself nor separate from it. End of quotation from Sheikh Muhammad al-Bitar.'
Source pages p. 19

Commentary
This passage is a marginal note by Sheikh Muhammad al-Bitar explaining the phrase from the main text: 'attributes that are neither the essence itself nor other than the essence.' He begins by stating that 'not identical' is obvious because an attribute is not the same as the thing it qualifies. Then he clarifies 'not other' in the technical sense: something that cannot be separated from the essence in existence. He quotes the poet from Bad’ al-Amalī: 'The attributes of Allah are not the essence itself, nor other than it that is separate.' The commentator then provides two proofs. First, if attributes were identical to the essence, there would be redundancy (tarāduf) between the name of the essence and its description, which is impossible. Second, if they were genuinely 'other' in the sense of separable, then other entities could be described by those attributes, implying that Allah would be deficient and perfect Himself through another—an absurdity. Al-Bitar then explains that 'other' in the phrase means separate in existence, not merely different in conceptual understanding. By consensus, the concept of the essence is different from the concept of the attributes, so in concept they are 'other,' but they are not separate in existence. He gives the example of the number one within ten: one is neither identical to ten nor separate from ten. Thus, the attributes are neither identical to the essence nor separable from it. This is the Ash'ari position on the relationship between essence and attributes.

Key Terms
لا عين ولا غير
الصفة
الذات
الترادف
الغيرين
الانفصال
بده الأمالي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that 'not identical and not other' means attributes are contradictory or nothing at all.
Correction: The phrase means attributes are distinct in concept from the essence (so not identical) but inseparable in existence (so not separate like two things). The analogy of one and ten clarifies this.

Checkpoint
What analogy is used in this passage to explain the relationship between Allah's attributes and His essence?
A) The relationship of fire to heat
B) The relationship of the number one to the number ten
C) The relationship of a king to his kingdom
D) The relationship of a body to its color

Key Takeaway
The attributes of Allah are distinct in concept from His essence but inseparable in existence, like the number one within ten.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Before this lesson, did you ever think of Allah's attributes as something separate from Him, or as just different ways of saying 'Allah'? Which of these two extremes were you closer to, and how does the one-in-ten analogy correct your mental model? In your next prostration, ask Allah to help you know Him as He truly is.
?
If you were to treat Allah's mercy or power as a 'thing' that could be separate from Him, how would that affect your trust in Him? Examine your heart: do you ever rely on means (like wealth, health, people) as if they hold mercy or power independently? Commit to redirecting your reliance solely to Allah, knowing His attributes are inseparably His.
?
The proof shows that if attributes were separate, Allah would be deficient in His essence and would need to perfect Himself through another. Have you ever found yourself thinking, even subtly, that Allah could be more 'complete' if He had certain attributes? How does this lesson expose that as a violation of tawhid? Write down one way you will actively affirm Allah's perfection in your daily life.

Allah: The Primitive Proper Noun
Definition of Allah and explanation of 'alam murtajal'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why 'Allah' is called a primitive proper noun and what it tells us about the divine essence.

Core Idea
'Allah' is a unique name not derived from any prior meaning.

The commentator begins by defining the greatest name of God, 'Allah', and explains its linguistic category as a primitive proper noun. This is not a mere grammatical detail; it underscores that the essence of Allah is not derived from any concept or attribute, but is the source of all attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of Allah
Read the Arabic text and translation for the definition of Allah. Notice the terms 'alam murtajal' and 'wajib al-wujud'.
Wajib al-wujud means Necessary Existence, a key philosophical term.
2
Understand 'alam murtajal'
Read the footnote explaining 'alam murtajal'. Contrast it with the transferred noun (manqul). Understand why this distinction matters for the name Allah.
3
Reflect on theological implications
Consider why the commentator insists that Allah is not derived from any prior meaning. How does this protect tawheed?
This guards against equating Allah with created concepts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و«الله»: عَلَم مرتجل على ذات واجب الوجود الموصوف بصفات الكمال المنزه عن سمات النقص. والرحمن الرحيم اسمان مشتقان من الرحمة وهي من صفات الله تعالى التي لا تدرك ولا تترك؛ فنؤمن بأن الله موصوف بالرحمة وننزهه تعالى عن معنى رقة القلب المفهومة من لفظ الرحمة عندنا كما هو مذهب السلف. والرحمن أعم رحمة من الرحيم؛ لشموله المؤمن والكافر والمطيع والعاصي والمكلف وغيره في الدنيا والآخرة، بخلاف رحمة الرحيم فإنها مخصوصة بالمؤمنين؛ قال تعالى: {وَكَانَ بِالْمُؤْمِنِينَ رَحِيمًا} (سورة الأحزاب آية: ٤٣)، وفيه نزول للأمر الإلهي بالتخصيص؛ فالله الجامع للصفات كلها، والرحمن نزول الأوصاف كلها مخصصة بالرحمة والرحيم تخصيص ثالث بالمؤمنين؛ ففي كلامه التدلي من جهة الأمر الإلهي والترقي من جهتنا، كذا في المطالب الوفية للعارف سيدي عبدالغني (٢) مع بعض اختصار.
(۱) العلم المرتجل: هو العلم الذي استعمل من أول أمره علماء كـ "سعاد" علم على امرأة، وهو قسيم المنقول والعلم المنقول هو ما نقل من شيء سبق استعماله فيه قبل العلمية؛ نحو المنقول من مصدر كـ" فضل" أو اسم عين كـ"أسد" أو اسم مفعول كـ "مسعود". انظر: شرح الأشموني على الفية ابن مالك (۱/ ۱۳۰).
And 'Allah' is a primitive proper noun for the essence of the Necessary Existence, described with attributes of perfection, transcendent of signs of deficiency. And 'al-Rahman' and 'al-Rahim' are two names derived from mercy (rahma), and it is one of the attributes of Allah, Exalted, which is not perceived nor abandoned; so we believe that Allah is described with mercy, and we transcend Him above the meaning of tenderness of heart understood from the word mercy among us, as is the madhhab of the Salaf. And al-Rahman is more general in mercy than al-Rahim, because it encompasses the believer and the disbeliever, the obedient and the disobedient, the morally obligated and others, in this world and the Hereafter, unlike the mercy of al-Rahim, for it is specific to the believers. Allah said: {And He is ever, to the believers, Merciful} (Surah al-Ahzab, verse 43), and in it is a descent of the divine command of specification. So Allah is the one who encompasses all attributes; al-Rahman is the descent of all attributes specified with mercy; and al-Rahim is a third specification to the believers. So in His speech is descent from the divine command and ascent from our side. Thus it is in al-Matalib al-Wafiyyah of the knower Sayyidi Abd al-Ghani (2) with some abridgment.
(1) The primitive proper noun: it is the noun that was used from its first instance as a proper noun, like 'Su'ad' as a proper noun for a woman, and it is opposite of the transferred noun. The transferred noun is what is transferred from something that was used before it became a proper noun; like the transferred from a masdar as 'Fadl', or from a concrete noun like 'Asad', or from a passive participle like 'Mas'ud'. See: Sharh al-Ashmuni on Alfiyyat Ibn Malik (1/130).
Source pages p. 20

Commentary
This passage is a theological definition of the divine names. First, 'Allah' is defined as a primitive proper noun (alam murtajal) for the essence of the Necessary Existence (wajib al-wujud), qualified with perfect attributes and devoid of any imperfection. Then, 'Rahman' and 'Rahim' are derived from rahma (mercy), which is an attribute affirmed for Allah but not in the human sense of sentiment; the Salaf affirm the attribute while negating any similarity. The key doctrinal point is the distinction: Rahman's mercy is general, including all creatures in this world and the Hereafter; Rahim's mercy is exclusive to believers, as confirmed by the verse from al-Ahzab. The commentator then explains a descending sequence: Allah possesses all attributes, Rahman descends to specify mercy, Rahim specifies further to believers. This reflects the divine command descending and our understanding ascending. The passage ends with a reference to al-Matalib al-Wafiyyah. The footnote defines the linguistic term 'alam murtajal' as a proper noun that is not transferred from a prior meaning.

Key Terms
الله
العلم المرتجل
واجب الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'Allah' is derived from 'ilah' (god), making it an ordinary derived name.
Correction: The commentator clarifies it is 'murtajal', not derived, emphasizing its unique, unborrowed nature.

Checkpoint
What is the meaning of 'alam murtajal' according to the text?
A) A noun derived from a prior meaning
B) A proper noun used from its first instance
C) A noun transferred from a common noun
D) A synonym for Allah

Key Takeaway
The name 'Allah' is a primitive proper noun, not derived from any prior concept, affirming the uniqueness of the divine essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allah' in your prayers, do you realize that you are calling upon a unique being with a name that has no origin in any human language? How does this deepen your sense of awe and directness in your relationship with Him?
?
If 'Allah' were a derived name (like 'the Worshipped One'), it might suggest a relationship to creation. But it is primitive — He is who He is, independent of our worship. Does your heart truly accept that Allah needs nothing from you, yet you are commanded to worship Him? How can this truth change the way you approach your acts of worship?
?
The name 'Allah' is not transferred from any prior concept, meaning He is not like anything we can imagine or compare. In what areas of your life are you tempted to form a mental image of Allah that suits your desires? What step will you take today to purify your understanding of His uniqueness?

The Mercy of Rahman and Rahim
Explanation of the derivation and scope of Rahman and Rahim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between Rahman and Rahim and the Salafi method of affirming divine attributes without anthropomorphism.

Core Idea
Rahman's mercy is universal; Rahim's mercy is for believers only.

After defining the name Allah, the commentator explains two of His most frequent names, Rahman and Rahim. Both derive from mercy, but they differ in scope. This lesson unpacks that difference and shows how the Salaf affirm mercy while rejecting human-like emotions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on Rahman and Rahim
Read the Arabic and translation. Pay attention to the derivation from 'rahma' and the distinction in scope.
Note the Quranic verse quoted as evidence.
2
Understand the Salafi stance on mercy
The text says we believe Allah has mercy but not the human tenderness of heart. This is the Salafi method of tafwid (consigning the modality to Allah).
3
Grasp the descending sequence
The commentator describes a sequence: Allah encompasses all attributes, Rahman specializes to mercy, Rahim further specifies to believers. This is a conceptual descent from the divine command.
This is a sophisticated theological concept.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و«الله»: عَلَم مرتجل على ذات واجب الوجود الموصوف بصفات الكمال المنزه عن سمات النقص. والرحمن الرحيم اسمان مشتقان من الرحمة وهي من صفات الله تعالى التي لا تدرك ولا تترك؛ فنؤمن بأن الله موصوف بالرحمة وننزهه تعالى عن معنى رقة القلب المفهومة من لفظ الرحمة عندنا كما هو مذهب السلف. والرحمن أعم رحمة من الرحيم؛ لشموله المؤمن والكافر والمطيع والعاصي والمكلف وغيره في الدنيا والآخرة، بخلاف رحمة الرحيم فإنها مخصوصة بالمؤمنين؛ قال تعالى: {وَكَانَ بِالْمُؤْمِنِينَ رَحِيمًا} (سورة الأحزاب آية: ٤٣)، وفيه نزول للأمر الإلهي بالتخصيص؛ فالله الجامع للصفات كلها، والرحمن نزول الأوصاف كلها مخصصة بالرحمة والرحيم تخصيص ثالث بالمؤمنين؛ ففي كلامه التدلي من جهة الأمر الإلهي والترقي من جهتنا، كذا في المطالب الوفية للعارف سيدي عبدالغني (٢) مع بعض اختصار.
(۱) العلم المرتجل: هو العلم الذي استعمل من أول أمره علماء كـ "سعاد" علم على امرأة، وهو قسيم المنقول والعلم المنقول هو ما نقل من شيء سبق استعماله فيه قبل العلمية؛ نحو المنقول من مصدر كـ" فضل" أو اسم عين كـ"أسد" أو اسم مفعول كـ "مسعود". انظر: شرح الأشموني على الفية ابن مالك (۱/ ۱۳۰).
And 'Allah' is a primitive proper noun for the essence of the Necessary Existence, described with attributes of perfection, transcendent of signs of deficiency. And 'al-Rahman' and 'al-Rahim' are two names derived from mercy (rahma), and it is one of the attributes of Allah, Exalted, which is not perceived nor abandoned; so we believe that Allah is described with mercy, and we transcend Him above the meaning of tenderness of heart understood from the word mercy among us, as is the madhhab of the Salaf. And al-Rahman is more general in mercy than al-Rahim, because it encompasses the believer and the disbeliever, the obedient and the disobedient, the morally obligated and others, in this world and the Hereafter, unlike the mercy of al-Rahim, for it is specific to the believers. Allah said: {And He is ever, to the believers, Merciful} (Surah al-Ahzab, verse 43), and in it is a descent of the divine command of specification. So Allah is the one who encompasses all attributes; al-Rahman is the descent of all attributes specified with mercy; and al-Rahim is a third specification to the believers. So in His speech is descent from the divine command and ascent from our side. Thus it is in al-Matalib al-Wafiyyah of the knower Sayyidi Abd al-Ghani (2) with some abridgment.
(1) The primitive proper noun: it is the noun that was used from its first instance as a proper noun, like 'Su'ad' as a proper noun for a woman, and it is opposite of the transferred noun. The transferred noun is what is transferred from something that was used before it became a proper noun; like the transferred from a masdar as 'Fadl', or from a concrete noun like 'Asad', or from a passive participle like 'Mas'ud'. See: Sharh al-Ashmuni on Alfiyyat Ibn Malik (1/130).

Commentary
This passage is a theological definition of the divine names. First, 'Allah' is defined as a primitive proper noun (alam murtajal) for the essence of the Necessary Existence (wajib al-wujud), qualified with perfect attributes and devoid of any imperfection. Then, 'Rahman' and 'Rahim' are derived from rahma (mercy), which is an attribute affirmed for Allah but not in the human sense of sentiment; the Salaf affirm the attribute while negating any similarity. Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Rahman and Rahim are synonyms with no difference in meaning.
Correction: The text explicitly says Rahman is more general, Rahim is specific to believers.

Checkpoint
According to the text, who is included in the mercy of al-Rahim?
A) All believers and disbelievers
B) Only believers
C) Only the obedient
D) All creatures

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How does the distinction between Rahman's universal mercy and Rahim's specific mercy challenge your assumptions about God's relationship with non-believers? Do you truly believe that God's mercy extends to them in this life, or do you secretly wish it didn't?
?
The passage teaches that divine mercy is not like human tenderness of heart. Have you ever reduced God's mercy to a sentimental feeling, expecting Him to overlook your sins without transformation? What must change in your understanding today?
?
If Rahim's mercy is exclusive to believers, what does that imply about your own standing before God? Are you truly among the believers who receive that specific mercy, or are you relying on general mercy while neglecting faith and obedience?

Divine Attributes

The Fourfold Classification of Divine Attributes
Imam Maydani classifies divine attributes into four categories with logical criteria.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how divine attributes are classified into four logical categories and the reasoning behind each category.

Core Idea
Every divine attribute fits into one of four logical categories: self, negative, meaning, or relational.

Imam Maydani presents a systematic classification of Allah's attributes, dividing them into four types based on logical criteria. This taxonomy helps students of kalam understand how each attribute relates to the Divine Essence. Before examining individual attributes, we need this conceptual framework.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four categories
Read the passage and list the four types: nafsiyya (self), salbiyya (negative), ma'ani (meanings), and ma'nawiyya (relational).
These are the four divisions of divine attributes according to the author.
2
Understand the branching logic
Trace the logical division: first, attributes either signify non-existence (salbiyya) or not. Those that don't are either existent (ma'ani) or not existent. Those not existent either indicate the essence alone (nafsiyya) or the essence with an added meaning (ma'nawiyya).
This shows rational categorization, not arbitrary.
3
See examples for each category
For nafsiyya, the example is existence (wujud). For salbiyya, unity (wahdaniyya) negates multiplicity. For ma'ani, knowledge and power. For ma'nawiyya, knowingness and powerfulness.
These examples clarify how each category functions.
4
Reflect on negative attributes
Note that negative attributes do not describe a positive quality but negate what is unbefitting to Allah, like negating multiplicity.
This addresses a common misunderstanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۲) اعلم أن الصفات من حيث هي منقسمة إلى أربعة أقسام: نفسية وسلبية ومعان ومعنوية: ووجه ذلك أن الصفة إما أن يكون مدلولها عدما - وهي السلبية. أو ليس مدلولها عدمًا، وهي إما موجودة، وهي صفات المعاني كالعلم والقدرة. أو ليست موجودة، وهي إما أن يدل الوصف بها على نفس الذات دون معنی زائد عليها، وهي النفسية أي الوجود. أو الوصف فيها يدل على نفس الذات مع معنى زائد عليها، وهي المعنوية كالعالمية والقادرية؛ أي كون الذات المتصفة بالعلم عالمة وكون الذات المتصفة بالقدرة قادرة. فمدلول السلبية سلب أمر لا يليق به سبحانه وتعالى كالوحدانية مدلولها سلب التعدد في الذات والصفات والأفعال أي نفيه. انظر شرح الدردير للخريدة ص (٤٣ - ٤٤)، وحاشية الصاوي على شرح الدردير ص (٣٤).
(2) Know that the attributes, in terms of themselves, are divided into four categories: self (nafsiyya), negative (salbiyya), meanings (maʿānī), and relational (maʿnawiyya). The explanation of that is that the attribute either its signification is a non-existence — and it is the negative; or its signification is not a non-existence, and it is either existent — and they are the attributes of meanings like knowledge and power; or not existent, and it is either that the description with it indicates the essence itself without an additional meaning upon it — and it is the self, i.e., existence; or the description in it indicates the essence itself with an additional meaning upon it — and it is the relational, like 'knowingness' (ʿālamiyya) and 'powerfulness' (qādriyya); i.e., that the essence described with knowledge is knowing and that the essence described with power is powerful. So the signification of the negative is the negation of something unbefitting to Him, Exalted and High, such as unity (al-waḥdāniyya), its signification is the negation of multiplicity in essence, attributes, and actions, i.e., its denial. See: Sharḥ al-Dardīr ʿalā al-Kharīda, pp. 43-44, and Ḥāshiyat al-Ṣāwī ʿalā Sharḥ al-Dardīr, p. 34.
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage is a classification of divine attributes (ṣifāt). The commentator, Imam Maydānī, states that attributes are divided into four categories: nafsiyya (self), salbiyya (negative), maʿānī (meanings), and maʿnawiyya (relational). He explains the reasoning: an attribute either signifies a non-existence (ʿadam) — that is the negative attribute (ṣifa salbiyya); or it does not signify a non-existence. In the latter case, it is either an existent entity (mawjūd), and these are the attributes of meanings (ṣifāt al-maʿānī) such as knowledge and power; or it is not an existent entity (i.e., it is a relation or description), and it is either that the description indicates the essence alone without an additional meaning — that is the self attribute (nafsiyya), which is existence itself (wujūd); or the description indicates the essence with an additional meaning upon it — that is the relational attribute (maʿnawiyya), like 'knowingness' (ʿālamiyya) and 'powerfulness' (qādriyya), meaning that the essence described with knowledge is 'knowing' and the essence described with power is 'powerful'. The passage concludes that the signification of a negative attribute is the negation of something unbefitting to Allah, such as waḥdāniyya (unity), which negates multiplicity in essence, attributes, and actions. The author then directs readers to further references: Sharḥ al-Dardīr ʿalā al-Kharīda and its gloss by al-Ṣāwī.

Key Terms
صفات (ṣifāt)
نفسية (nafsiyya)
سلبية (salbiyya)
معان (maʿānī)
معنوية (maʿnawiyya)
وجود (wujūd)
عالمية (ʿālamiyya)
قادرية (qādriyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Negative attributes are not real attributes because they only negate.
Correction: The passage affirms that negative attributes are a valid category of divine attributes; they are as integral as positive ones, as they describe what Allah is not.

Checkpoint
According to the classification in this passage, which category of divine attributes includes attributes like knowledge and power?
A) Self attributes (nafsiyya)
B) Negative attributes (salbiyya)
C) Attributes of meanings (maʿānī)
D) Relational attributes (maʿnawiyya)

Key Takeaway
Divine attributes are logically categorized into four types: self, negative, meaning, and relational, providing a systematic way to understand how each describes Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Which of these four categories of Allah’s attributes have you most overlooked in your personal reflection? How will you intentionally include the neglected ones in your worship starting today?
?
The self attribute 'existence' is foundational. In what area of your life do you act as if Allah’s existence is not enough, and what step will you take to rely solely on His existence for that matter?
?
Relational attributes like 'knowingness' imply that Allah’s knowledge is inseparable from His essence. How does this reality confront your secret sin or hidden doubt, and what practical change will you make tonight to align with His all-knowing presence?

The Four Types of Divine Attributes
Classification of divine attributes into four types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four types of divine attributes in Ash'ari theology and how they are distinguished based on their denotation.

Core Idea
Divine attributes fall into four logical types: essential, negative, of meanings, and meaningful.

This passage from Imam al-Maydani's commentary presents a foundational classification that every student of Islamic theology must grasp. The classification is based on a simple logical question: does the attribute denote a non-existence or something positive? From here, four categories emerge. Understanding this taxonomy is key to making sense of later discussions about God's names and attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the author uses a binary division: first between absence and non-absence, then within non-absence between existent and non-existent.
2
Understand the division logic
Trace the logical tree: Attribute → either its denotation is an absence (negative) or not. If not, either it is an existent attribute (ma'ani) or not. If not existent, either it indicates the essence alone (nafsiyya) or with an added meaning (ma'nawiyya).
This tree is the key to the classification.
3
Identify examples for each type
Find in the passage: Negative (salbiyya) example is Oneness (al-wahdaniyya) which negates multiplicity. Attribute of meaning (ma'ani) example is knowledge and power. Essential (nafsiyya) example is existence. Meaningful (ma'nawiyya) example is 'being knowledgeable' (al-'alimiyya).
Notice the subtle distinction between ma'ani (the attribute itself) and ma'nawiyya (the description derived from it).
4
Reflect on the importance
Why do we need this classification? It prevents confusion about whether attributes are real or merely relational. Negative attributes are real descriptions even though they deny something. This taxonomy is foundational for later discussions on God's attributes.
The author himself points out that negative attributes still have a real denotation (negation of something unbefitting).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعلم أن الصفات من حيث هي منقسمة إلى أربعة أقسام: نفسية وسلبية ومعان ومعنوية: ووجه ذلك أن الصفة إما أن يكون مدلولها عدما - وهي السلبية. أو ليس مدلولها عدمًا، وهي إما موجودة، وهي صفات المعاني كالعلم والقدرة. أو ليست موجودة، وهي إما أن يدل الوصف بها على نفس الذات دون معنى زائد عليها، وهي النفسية أي الوجود. أو الوصف فيها يدل على نفس الذات مع معنى زائد عليها، وهي المعنوية كالعالمية والقادرية؛ أي كون الذات المتصفة بالعلم عالمة وكون الذات المتصفة بالقدرة قادرة. فمدلول السلبية سلب أمر لا يليق به سبحانه وتعالى كالوحدانية مدلولها سلب التعدد في الذات والصفات والأفعال أي نفيه. انظر شرح الدردير للخريدة ص (43-44)، وحاشية الصاوي على شرح الدردير ص (34).
Know that attributes, as such, are divided into four sections: essential (nafsiyya), negative (salbiyya), attributes of meanings (ma'ani), and meaningful attributes (ma'nawiyya). And the explanation of that is that the attribute is either such that its denotation is a non-existence (absence) — and it is the negative. Or its denotation is not a non-existence, and it is either existent, which are the attributes of meanings like knowledge and power. Or it is not existent, and it is either that the description by it indicates the essence itself without an added meaning upon it, and it is the essential, i.e., existence. Or the description in it indicates the essence itself with an added meaning upon it, and it is the meaningful attributes like being knowledgeable (al-'alimiyya) and being capable (al-qadiriyya); i.e., the essence qualified by knowledge being knowing and the essence qualified by power being capable. Thus the denotation of the negative is the negation of something unbefitting to Him, Exalted and High, such as Oneness: its denotation is the negation of multiplicity in essence, attributes, and actions, i.e., its denial. See: Sharh al-Dardir li al-Kharida p. (43-44), and Hashiya al-Sawi on Sharh al-Dardir p. (34).
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage is a classification of divine attributes into four types. The author first states that attributes are divided into four: essential (nafsiyya), negative (salbiyya), attributes of meanings (ma'ani), and meaningful attributes (ma'nawiyya). He then explains the rationale: an attribute is either such that its content (madlul) is an absence (like negation) — that is the negative. If its content is not an absence, then either it is an existent attribute (like knowledge and power, which are real entities subsisting in the essence) — these are attributes of meanings. Or it is not an existent attribute (i.e., it is a relational or modal concept), and then either the description by it indicates the very self of the essence without any added meaning (like existence itself) — that is the essential attribute. Or the description indicates the essence with an added meaning (like being knowledgeable, which indicates the essence plus the attribute of knowledge) — that is the meaningful attribute. He then applies this: the negative attribute negates something unbefitting to Allah, as Oneness negates multiplicity in essence, attributes, and actions. This classification is foundational for Ash'ari theology.

Key Terms
نفسية
سلبية
معاني
معنوية
صفات
مدلول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Negative attributes are not real attributes because they only negate.
Correction: Negative attributes are real descriptions of God; they have a real denotation (the negation of an unbefitting thing). For example, Oneness is a real attribute that negates multiplicity.

Checkpoint
Which type of attribute does 'Oneness' (الوحدانية) belong to?
A) Essential (نفسية).
B) Negative (سلبية).
C) Attribute of meaning (معنى).
D) Meaningful (معنوية).

Key Takeaway
The classification of attributes into nafsiyya, salbiyya, ma'ani, and ma'nawiyya is based on the logical nature of their denotation, and it is essential for correct understanding of God's attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allah is One,' do you merely utter it, or do you truly deny all multiplicity in His essence? Reflect on how often you attribute parts or causes to Him in your heart without realizing it. Will you now actively fight those subtle shirk thoughts?
?
Do you truly believe that Allah's attributes like knowledge and power are real and perfect, not just concepts? When you pray, do you call upon Him with these attributes trusting their reality? Or are your prayers hollow because you haven't internalized that His knowledge is actual?
?
Consider the attribute of existence (nafsiyya). Does your awareness of Allah's existence affect your daily actions? Do you live as if He is the only truly Existent? What would change if you truly felt that He alone exists and all else depends on Him?

Allah's Incapability is Impossible and the Reality of Incapability
Negates incapacity from Allah and defines incapability as an existent attribute opposing power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why incapacity cannot be attributed to Allah and how Ahl al-Sunna define incapability as an existent attribute opposite to power.

Core Idea
Incapability is a real attribute opposite to power, but it is impossible for Allah.

The commentary elaborates on the phrase 'and nothing incapacitates Him' from the Tahawiyyah creed. It affirms Allah's absolute power and clarifies that incapability (al-ajz) is not merely the absence of power but an actual attribute that contradicts power. This is a key distinction from the Mu'tazila who deny the existence of such attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the phrase 'and nothing incapacitates Him' and the definition of incapability.
The passage is short but dense. Read it carefully.
2
Understand the negation of incapacity
The phrase 'nothing incapacitates Him' means that Allah is able to do anything possible, whether bringing into existence or annihilating. No act is beyond His power.
3
Learn the definition of incapability
Incapability (al-ajz) is defined as an attribute with which one cannot create or annihilate anything. It is a real quality, not just a lack of ability.
4
Note the Sunni position on its nature
According to Ahl al-Sunna, incapability is an 'existent matter' (amr wujudi) – a real attribute that is opposite to power. This opposes the Mu'tazila view that it is merely a negation.
This is a key theological distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم أكد ذلك بقوله: (ولا شيء يعجزه) عن فعل ممكن ما وجودًا وعدمًا، والعجز صفة لا يتأتى معها إيجاد شيء ولا إعدامه، وهو أمر وجودي على مذهب أهل السنة يضاد القدرة؛ كذا في شرح السنوسية. (شرح الهدهدي للسنوسية بحاشية الشرقاوي ص (90).)
Then he confirmed that by his saying: (and nothing incapacitates Him) from any possible act, whether existence or non-existence; and incapability (al-ajz) is an attribute with which the bringing into existence of anything nor its annihilation is not possible, and it is an existent matter (amr wujudi) according to the doctrine of Ahl al-Sunna, opposing power (al-qudra); thus in Sharh al-Sanusiyya. (Sharh al-Hududi li al-Sanusiyya bi hashiat al-Sharqawi p. (90).)
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage explains the creedal statement "and nothing incapacitates Him" (wa la shay' yu'jazuhu). The author says this phrase confirms the previous affirmation of oneness. The meaning is that no possible act, whether bringing into existence or annihilating, can be beyond His ability. He then defines incapability (al-ajz): it is an attribute with which one cannot create or annihilate anything. According to the school of Ahl al-Sunna, incapability is an existent matter (amr wujudi), meaning it is a real attribute that is opposite to power (al-qudra). This is opposed to the Mu'tazila who might consider it a mere negation. The author refers to Sharh al-Sanusiyya for further confirmation. This reinforces that Allah has absolute power without any defect.

Key Terms
عجز
قدرة
إيجاد
إعدام
أمر وجودي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Incapability is merely the absence or negation of power.
Correction: According to Ahl al-Sunna, incapability is an existent attribute (amr wujudi) that is opposite to power, not just a lack of power.

Checkpoint
According to Ahl al-Sunna, what is the nature of incapability (العجز)?
A) A negation of power.
B) An existent attribute that opposes power.
C) A potentiality.
D) A lack of ability.

Key Takeaway
Incapability is a real attribute opposite to power, but it is impossible for Allah, affirming His absolute power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a situation that feels impossible, do you truly believe that nothing is impossible for Allah? How does this lesson's assertion that 'nothing incapacitates Him' challenge your reliance on human solutions?
?
Incapability is defined as an existent attribute that prevents action. Reflect on a time you were incapable of something. How does recognizing that Allah has no trace of this attribute change your trust in His power over your life?
?
The passage distinguishes between Allah and created beings by denying al-ajz from Allah. Are there areas in your life where you act as if Allah is limited? What would it mean for you to truly live with the conviction that He is incapable of nothing?

The Three Categories of Divine Unity
Three categories of tawhid: essence, attributes, actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three categories of divine unity as defined in classical Islamic theology: unity of essence, unity of attributes, and unity of actions.

Core Idea
Tawhid means Allah is one in essence, unique in every attribute, and sole creator.

This passage from a classical commentary clarifies that tawhid is not a single concept but has three dimensions. Before we can properly affirm Allah's oneness, we must understand how He is one in His being, His qualities, and His actions. The author enumerates each category with a clear definition, grounding our understanding of Islamic monotheism.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice how the author structures the three categories with 'الأول', 'والثاني', 'والثالث'.
The passage ends with a citation to Sharh al-Tariqa.
2
Identify each type of unity
For each category, write down its definition in your own words. Focus on what is negated (multiplicity, likeness, partnership) and what is affirmed (oneness, uniqueness, exclusivity).
3
Reflect on the practical implication
Consider why these distinctions matter for worship and theology. How does belief in unity of actions affect how we view cause and effect in the world?
This connects tawhid to daily life and reliance on Allah alone.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول: الوحدة في الذات والمراد بها انتفاء الكثرة عن ذاته تعالى بمعنى عدم قبولها الانقسام. والثاني: الوحدة في الصفات والمراد بها انتفاء النظير له تعالى في كل صفة من صفاته؛ فيمتنع أن يكون له تعالى علوم وقدرات متكثرة بحسب المعلومات والمقدورات بل علمه تعالى واحد ومعلوماته كثيرة وقدرته واحدة ومقدوراته كثيرة، وعلى هذا جميع صفاته. والثالث: الوحدة في الأفعال، والمراد بها انفراده تعالى باختراع جميع الكائنات عموما، وامتناع إسناد التأثير لغيره تعالى في شيء من الممكنات أصلا. كذا في شرح الطريقة.
First: unity in essence, meaning the negation of multiplicity from His essence, i.e., not accepting division. Second: unity in attributes, meaning the negation of any likeness to Him in every attribute; it is impossible for Him to have multiple knowledges and powers according to the numerous things known and things capable, rather His knowledge is one and His objects of knowledge are many, His power is one and His objects of power are many, and similarly for all His attributes. Third: unity in actions, meaning His exclusive creation of all existents in general, and the impossibility of attributing any effect to other than Him in any possible thing at all. Thus in Sharh al-Tariqa.
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage enumerates three categories of tawhid (oneness) with respect to Allah. The author first defines unity of essence (al-wahda fi al-dhat), which means that Allah’s essence is not divisible; there is no multiplicity in His being. Second, unity of attributes (al-wahda fi al-sifat) means that each of Allah’s attributes is unique and without peer; for example, His knowledge is one and encompasses all objects of knowledge, and His power is one over all objects of power. There cannot be multiple knowledges or powers within Allah. Third, unity of actions (al-wahda fi al-af'al) means that Allah alone is the creator of all things, and no effect in the universe can be ascribed to any other being. The passage ends by citing the commentary Sharh al-Tariqa as its source.

Key Terms
الوحدة في الذات
الوحدة في الصفات
الوحدة في الأفعال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think tawhid only means no partners in essence, but it also encompasses uniqueness in attributes and actions.
Correction: Tawhid includes all three categories: essence, attributes, and actions, as explained in the passage.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does 'unity in attributes' (al-wahda fi al-sifat) mean?
A) Allah has multiple attributes but each is divisible
B) There is no likeness to Allah in any of His attributes
C) Allah's attributes are separate from His essence
D) Allah's attributes are many like His objects

Key Takeaway
The three categories of unity — essence, attributes, and actions — form a complete understanding of tawhid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you succeed in a task, do you instinctively credit your own ability, or do you pause to recognize that Allah alone created every cause and effect? How can you train your heart to see His exclusive action in every outcome?
?
If Allah's knowledge is one and encompasses all things, including your hidden thoughts and future sins, how should this transform your private moments? Will you live today as if His all-encompassing knowledge sees every intention?
?
You affirm that Allah has no peers, but do you ever treat His mercy and justice as if they are separate forces? How can you unify your understanding of His attributes so that you neither despair nor become complacent?

The Nature of Divine Incapacity
Author defines incapacity and affirms it as a positive attribute opposing power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that in Sunni theology, incapacity ('ajz) is considered a real, positive attribute opposed to divine power, not merely the absence of power.

Core Idea
Incapacity is not just lack of power — it is a real attribute.

This lesson examines a key theological distinction between Sunni Islam and other groups regarding the nature of incapacity. The passage clarifies that when we say 'nothing incapacitates Allah,' it implies that incapacity itself is a positive attribute that cannot apply to Him. Let's read the source passage to see the argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic carefully, then the literal translation. Notice the key terms 'العجز', 'أمر وجودي', and 'القدرة'.
2
Define incapacity according to the text
Identify how the passage defines 'ajz: as an attribute with which no creation or annihilation is possible.
3
Understand the sectarian point
The passage states that incapacity is an 'amr wujudi' (existent thing) according to Ahl al-Sunna, opposing power. This is a response to the Mu'tazila who considered it non-existent.
Contrast with 'amr 'adami' (non-existent).
4
Reflect on the implication for divine attributes
If incapacity is a real attribute opposed to power, then Allah's power is absolute because He has no such attribute. This defends tawhid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم أكد ذلك بقوله: (ولا شيء يعجزه عن فعل ممكن ما وجودًا وعدمًا، والعجز صفة لا يتأتى معها إيجاد شيء ولا إعدامه، وهو أمر وجودي على مذهب أهل السنة يضاد القدرة؛ كذا في شرح السنوسية
Then He affirmed that by His saying: 'And nothing incapacitates Him from any possible act, whether in existence or non-existence.' And incapacity is an attribute with which neither bringing into existence nor annihilating anything is attainable, and it is an existent thing (amr wujudi) according to the school of Ahl al-Sunna, opposing power. Thus in Sharh al-Sanusiyya.
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage explains the Qur'anic statement 'nor does anything incapacitate Him' (wa la shay' yu'jizuhu) by defining incapacity ('ajz). Incapacity is an attribute that prevents the one possessing it from creating or destroying anything. The author then clarifies a theological point: incapacity is a positive (wujudi) attribute, meaning it has real existence, according to Ahl al-Sunna, and it is opposed to power (qudra). This is a key difference from the Mu'tazila, some of whom considered incapacity to be merely the absence of power (amr 'adami). The passage concludes by citing Sharh al-Sanusiyya as its source.

Key Terms
العجز
أمر وجودي
القدرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think incapacity is merely the absence of power (amr 'adami).
Correction: The passage affirms that incapacity is an existent thing (amr wujudi) opposed to power, so it is a real attribute, not just a lack.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the nature of incapacity ('ajz) on the school of Ahl al-Sunna?
A) A non-existent thing (amr 'adami)
B) An existent thing (amr wujudi) opposing power
C) A mixture of existence and non-existence
D) Not an attribute at all

Key Takeaway
Incapacity is a positive attribute opposed to power; therefore, Allah's power is absolute and nothing can render Him incapable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When was the last time you felt completely powerless? Did you truly believe that nothing can incapacitate Allah, or did you act as if your situation was too difficult for Him? What would change if you lived with the certainty that nothing renders Him incapable?
?
The passage defines incapacity as an attribute that prevents action. You are a creature, not the Creator—your incapacity is real and profound. Are you honest with yourself about your limits, or do you live in denial of your weakness? How does acknowledging your own 'ajz drive you to reliance on the One who has qudra?
?
If incapacity is an existent thing according to the scholars, then your inability to accomplish something is not just a lack—it is a real state of being. When you face a task beyond your strength, do you turn to Allah in recognition of your own real weakness, or do you despair or boast? Pledge today one practical way you will acknowledge your dependence on Allah's limitless power.

Definition and Proof of God's Pre-eternity (Qidam)
Definition and proof of God's pre-eternity (qidam).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meaning of God's pre-eternity (qidam) as essential beginninglessness and why it is rationally necessary, avoiding the impossibilities of circularity and infinite regress.

Core Idea
God's existence has no beginning; otherwise, impossible circularity or infinite regress would follow.

The commentator first defines the attribute of pre-eternity, stating that Allah is Eternal (qadim) without beginning. He then provides a proof: if His existence had a beginning, it would require circularity or infinite regress, both of which are impossible. This lesson unpacks that definition and proof, and also clarifies distinctions between essential, temporal, and relative eternality.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the Arabic definition and proof once without analyzing. Let the author's own rhythm come through.
2
Identify the definition and proof
Notice that 'qadim' is defined as 'without beginning', and the proof is the impossibility of being preceded by non-existence.
3
Understand the exclusions
The commentator restricts 'essential' to exclude temporal and relative eternality, which are not attributes of God.
4
Distinguish between 'qadim' and 'azali'
Learn that 'qadim' applies only to existents, while 'azali' can apply to both existents and non-existents.
This distinction is unique to theological terminology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القدم والبقاء] قَدِيمُ بِلَا ابْتِدَاءٍ دَائِمٌ بِلَا انْتِهَاءٍ، لَا يَفْنَى وَلَا يَبِيدُ. (قديم) قدمًا ذاتيا (بلا ابتداء) أي ليس مسبوقاً بعدم وإلا لزم الدور أو التسلسل وكلاهما محال كما هو مقرر. وخرج بقيد الذاتي القدم الزماني كأمس بالنسبة لليوم والإضافي كالأب بالنسبة لولده، والقدم صفة سلبية أخص من الأزل لأن القديم موجود لا أول له، والأزلي ما لا أول له أعم من أن يكون وجوديًا كذات مولانا عز وجل أو عدميا كعدمنا الأزلي. (دائم) أي باقي (بلا انتهاء) أي ليس ملحوقًا بعدم؛ المعبر عنه بامتناع طروء العدم على وجوده تعالى؛ لأن من ثبت قدمه استحال عدمه، والبقاء صفة سلبية أيضًا، وقد أردفها على طريق التفسير والتأكيد بقوله (لا يفنى) أي لا يزول بقاؤه، يقال: فنى الميت إذا زال وذهب أثره. مختار. (ولا يبيد) أي لا ينقطع بقاؤه؛ يقال: بادت القبيلة؛ إذا انقطعت. مختار. (١) الدور هو توقف الشيء على ما يتوقف عليه من جهة واحدة؛ كما يتوقف (أ) على حصول (ب) و (ب) على حصول (أ)، وله أنواع: الأول: دور معي: مثاله: توقف تعقل الأبوة على تعقل البنوة وبالعكس، وهذا القسم ليس بمستحيل؛ لأن الإضافات أمور اعتبارية لا وجود لها إلا في الأذهان. الثاني: الدور السبقي وهو توقف وجود شيء على وجود شيء آخر قد توقف هذا الشيء الآخر عليه، وهذا القسم مستحيل لأنه لو توقف كل واحد منهما على الآخر لافتقر الشيء إلى نفسه وافتقار الشيء إلى نفسه محال. والتسلسل أن يتلاقى معروض العلة والمعلولية في سلسلة واحدة من معلول معين إلى غير نهاية بأن يستند الممكن في وجوده إلى علة مؤثرة وتستند تلك العلة المؤثرة إلى علة مؤثرة وهلم جرا إلى غير نهاية، ولقد ذكر علماء الكلام عدة أدلة على بطلان التسلسل منها برهان التطبيق وبرهان التضايف. انظر: تحرير القواعد المنطقية للقطب الرازي ص (١٤)، وشرح المقاصد (٢/ ١١١)، والقول السديد في علم التوحيد للشيخ محمود أبو دقيقة (١٦٢/١-١٦٦)، والتحقيق التام في علم الكلام لمحمد الحسيني الظواهري ص (٤٠). (٢) مختار الصحاح مادة (فني). (٣) مختار الصحاح، مادة (بيد).
Pre-eternity and Everlastingness: He is Eternal without beginning, Everlasting without end, He does not perish nor cease. (Eternal) with essential eternality (without beginning) meaning not preceded by non-existence, otherwise circularity or infinite regress would be necessary, both of which are impossible as established. By the restriction 'essential' there is excluded temporal eternality like yesterday relative to today, and relative eternality like the father relative to his son. And eternality is a negative attribute more specific than pre-eternity (azal) because the Eternal (qadim) is an existent with no first, and the pre-eternal (azali) is that which has no first, more general, whether existential like the essence of our Lord, may He be exalted, or non-existential like our pre-eternal non-existence. (Everlasting) meaning remaining (without end) meaning not overtaken by non-existence; expressed as the impossibility of non-existence befalling His existence, because whoever is proven to be Eternal (qadim) it is impossible for him to cease. And everlastingness is also a negative attribute, and He appended to it by way of explanation and confirmation His statement (He does not perish) meaning His remaining does not vanish; it is said: the deceased perishes when his effect vanishes and goes away. Mukhtar. (And does not cease) meaning His remaining is not cut off; it is said: the tribe ceases when it is cut off. Mukhtar. (1) Circularity is the dependence of a thing on that upon which it depends from one aspect; such as (A) depending on the existence of (B) and (B) depending on the existence of (A). It has types: first: concurrent circularity: example: the dependence of understanding fatherhood on understanding sonship and vice versa, and this type is not impossible because relations are mental matters that have no existence except in minds. Second: sequential circularity, which is the dependence of the existence of a thing on the existence of another thing which itself depends on the former; and this type is impossible because if each depended on the other, a thing would need itself, and a thing needing itself is impossible. And infinite regress is that the subject of cause and effect in a single chain from a particular effect goes on infinitely, such that the contingent depends in its existence on an effective cause, and that effective cause depends on another effective cause, and so on to infinity. The scholars of kalam have mentioned several proofs for the invalidity of infinite regress, including the proof of application and the proof of correlation. See: Tahrir al-Qawa'id al-Mantiqiyya by al-Qutb al-Razi p. 14, and Sharh al-Maqasid (2/111), and al-Qawl al-Sadid fi 'Ilm al-Tawhid by Shaykh Mahmud Abu Daqiqa (1/162-166), and al-Tahqiq al-Tamm fi 'Ilm al-Kalam by Muhammad al-Husayni al-Zawahiri p. 40. (2) Mukhtar al-Sihah, entry (fani). (3) Mukhtar al-Sihah, entry (bayda).
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage is a commentary explaining the attributes of God's pre-eternity (qidam) and everlastingness (baqa') from the Tahawiyyah creed. The commentator defines 'qadim' as essentially eternal without beginning, meaning that God's existence is not preceded by non-existence. If it were, that would necessitate either circularity (dawr) or infinite regress (tasalsul), both of which are impossible as established in kalam. He then refines the definition by noting that 'essential' (dhati) eternality excludes two other types: temporal eternality (qidam zamani), like yesterday relative to today, and relative eternality (qidam idafi), like a father relative to his son. Qidam is a negative attribute (sifa salbiyya) and is more specific than 'azal' (pre-eternity), because 'qadim' is an existent with no beginning, while 'azali' can apply to both existents (like God) and non-existents (like our pre-eternal non-existence). Next, he explains 'da'im' as everlasting without end, meaning that non-existence cannot happen to God's existence, because if something is proven to be eternal (qadim), its non-existence is impossible. Everlastingness (baqa') is also a negative attribute. The phrases 'la yafna' and 'la yabid' are confirmatory explanations: 'la yafna' means His remaining does not vanish, and 'la yabid' means His remaining is not cut off, citing lexical meanings from Mukhtar al-Sihah. The footnote, marked (1), provides a detailed explanation of the concepts of circularity (dawr) and infinite regress (tasalsul) used in the proof. It defines circularity as the dependence of a thing on what it itself depends on in one respect, giving the example of A needing B and B needing A. It distinguishes two types: concurrent circularity (dawr ma'i), which is not impossible because it involves mental relations (e.g., fatherhood and sonship), and sequential circularity (dawr sabqi), which is impossible because it would require a thing to depend on itself. Infinite regress (tasalsul) is defined as an endless chain of causes and effects, and the footnote notes that kalam scholars have provided proofs against it, such as the proof of application (burhan al-tatbiq) and the proof of correlation (burhan al-tadāyuf), citing several references. The entire passage serves to establish God's beginningless and endless existence as a rational necessity.

دور
تسلسل
صفة سلبية
أزل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking 'qadim' means old in time, like an ancient object.
Correction: In theology, 'qadim' means without beginning, and is a negative attribute, not a measure of age.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what type of eternality is excluded by the restriction 'essential' (dhati)?
A) Temporal eternality
B) Relative eternality
C) Both
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
God's pre-eternity (qidam) means He has no beginning, and this is a negative attribute that distinguishes Him from all temporal and relative beginnings.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God has no beginning, and you have a beginning, what does that say about your absolute dependence on Him in every moment? How does this truth shape your attitude towards your own mortality and your need for His eternal care? This week, consciously remind yourself each morning that you are not self-sufficient; you began and will end, but He never began.
?
The proof of God's pre-eternity eliminates the possibility of any other eternal being. Does your heart truly acknowledge that only God is without beginning, or do you secretly rely on something else as though it were eternal—your health, wealth, relationships, or reputation? What would it mean to uproot that false dependency today and declare with your life that only Allah is Qadim?
?
The attribute of qidam is a negative attribute: it tells you what God is not (not having a beginning). How does this change the way you think about God? Are you content to know Him through what He is not, or do you try to confine Him to human-like qualities? Spend time in worship this week focusing on God's transcendence, saying 'SubhanAllah' with the awareness that He is beyond all temporal limitations.

God's Everlastingness (Baqa') – No End to His Existence
Definition and proof of God's everlastingness (baqa').

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meaning of God's everlastingness (baqa') as endless existence and why it is a necessary consequence of His pre-eternity.

Core Idea
What has no beginning cannot cease to exist; God's existence is endless.

Having established that Allah is eternal without beginning, the commentator now explains that He is also everlasting without end. He argues that if something is proven to be eternal, its non-existence is impossible. This lesson covers the definition, proof, and confirmatory phrases.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage on everlastingness
Read the passage once without trying to analyze. Focus on the flow from 'da'im' to 'la yabid'.
2
Understand the definition and proof
Notice that 'da'im' is defined as without end, and the proof is 'because whoever is eternal, his non-existence is impossible'.
3
Identify everlastingness as a negative attribute
The commentator explicitly states that everlastingness (baqa') is also a negative attribute.
4
Examine the confirmatory phrases
The phrases 'la yafna' and 'la yabid' reinforce that God's remaining does not vanish or cease.
Lexical references from Mukhtar al-Sihah are cited.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Il testo dice che Dio non ha fine. Se tutto intorno a te – successi, relazioni, persino te stesso – è destinato a finire, cosa stai cercando disperatamente di trattenere che solo Dio può darti in modo permanente? Come cambierebbe la tua agenda settimanale se vivessi alla luce di questa verità?
?
La prova della continuità di Dio parte dalla Sua eternità. La tua esistenza ha un inizio, e avrà una fine. In che modo la certezza della tua mortalità influenza le tue scelte quotidiane? Stai vivendo come se questo mondo fosse la tua dimora definitiva o come un viaggiatore verso l'Incontro con Colui che non finisce?
?
L'attributo della continuità è negativo: nega che Dio possa cessare. Quali 'dei' temporanei – persone, ambizioni, sicurezze – stai inconsciamente trattando come se non potessero finire? Prendi un impegno oggi stesso per smascherare una di queste false sicurezze e riporre la tua speranza esclusivamente in Dio.

Circularity and Infinite Regress – The Logical Impossibilities
Detailed explanation of circularity and infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definitions of circularity (dawr) and infinite regress (tasalsul), their types, and why they are impossible, thereby supporting the proof of God's pre-eternity.

Core Idea
An endless chain of causes is impossible; there must be a First Cause without beginning.

The commentary's footnote provides a detailed explanation of the two impossibilities used in the proof of God's pre-eternity: circularity and infinite regress. Understanding these concepts is essential to grasping why the universe cannot exist without a beginningless Creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on dawr and tasalsul
Read the footnote carefully. It defines two key terms used in the proof of pre-eternity.
2
Understand circularity and its types
Note the definition of circularity and the distinction between concurrent and sequential circularity.
3
Understand infinite regress
Infinite regress is defined as an endless chain of causes and effects.
4
Learn why both are impossible
Sequential circularity is impossible because it requires self-dependence. Infinite regress is impossible, as proven by kalam arguments.
Refer to the cited proofs: burhan al-tatbiq and burhan al-tadāyuf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(قديم) قدمًا ذاتيا (بلا ابتداء) أي ليس مسبوقاً بعدم وإلا لزم الدور أو التسلسل وكلاهما محال كما هو مقرر. وخرج بقيد الذاتي القدم الزماني كأمس بالنسبة لليوم والإضافي كالأب بالنسبة لولده، والقدم صفة سلبية أخص من الأزل لأن القديم موجود لا أول له، والأزلي ما لا أول له أعم من أن يكون وجوديًا كذات مولانا عز وجل أو عدميا كعدمنا الأزلي. (دائم) أي باقي (بلا انتهاء) أي ليس ملحوقًا بعدم؛ المعبر عنه بامتناع طروء العدم على وجوده تعالى؛ لأن من ثبت قدمه استحال عدمه، والبقاء صفة سلبية أيضًا، وقد أردفها على طريق التفسير والتأكيد بقوله (لا يفنى) أي لا يزول بقاؤه، يقال: فنى الميت إذا زال وذهب أثره. مختار. (ولا يبيد) أي لا ينقطع بقاؤه؛ يقال: بادت القبيلة؛ إذا انقطعت. مختار. (١) الدور هو توقف الشيء على ما يتوقف عليه من جهة واحدة؛ كما يتوقف (أ) على حصول (ب) و (ب) على حصول (أ)، وله أنواع: الأول: دور معي: مثاله: توقف تعقل الأبوة على تعقل البنوة وبالعكس، وهذا القسم ليس بمستحيل؛ لأن الإضافات أمور اعتبارية لا وجود لها إلا في الأذهان. الثاني: الدور السبقي وهو توقف وجود شيء على وجود شيء آخر قد توقف هذا الشيء الآخر عليه، وهذا القسم مستحيل لأنه لو توقف كل واحد منهما على الآخر لافتقر الشيء إلى نفسه وافتقار الشيء إلى نفسه محال. والتسلسل أن يتلاقى معروض العلة والمعلولية في سلسلة واحدة من معلول معين إلى غير نهاية بأن يستند الممكن في وجوده إلى علة مؤثرة وتستند تلك العلة المؤثرة إلى علة مؤثرة وهلم جرا إلى غير نهاية، ولقد ذكر علماء الكلام عدة أدلة على بطلان التسلسل منها برهان التطبيق وبرهان التضايف. انظر: تحرير القواعد المنطقية للقطب الرازي ص (١٤)، وشرح المقاصد (٢/ ١١١)، والقول السديد في علم التوحيد للشيخ محمود أبو دقيقة (١٦٢/١-١٦٦)، والتحقيق التام في علم الكلام لمحمد الحسيني الظواهري ص (٤٠). (٢) مختار الصحاح مادة (فني). (٣) مختار الصحاح، مادة (بيد).
Pre-eternity and Everlastingness: He is Eternal without beginning, Everlasting without end, He does not perish nor cease. (Eternal) with essential eternality (without beginning) meaning not preceded by non-existence, otherwise circularity or infinite regress would be necessary, both of which are impossible as established. By the restriction 'essential' there is excluded temporal eternality like yesterday relative to today, and relative eternality like the father relative to his son. And eternality is a negative attribute more specific than pre-eternity (azal) because the Eternal (qadim) is an existent with no first, and the pre-eternal (azali) is that which has no first, more general, whether existential like the essence of our Lord, may He be exalted, or non-existential like our pre-eternal non-existence. (Everlasting) meaning remaining (without end) meaning not overtaken by non-existence; expressed as the impossibility of non-existence befalling His existence, because whoever is proven to be Eternal (qadim) it is impossible for him to cease. And everlastingness is also a negative attribute, and He appended to it by way of explanation and confirmation His statement (He does not perish) meaning His remaining does not vanish; it is said: the deceased perishes when his effect vanishes and goes away. Mukhtar. (And does not cease) meaning His remaining is not cut off; it is said: the tribe ceases when it is cut off. Mukhtar. (1) Circularity is the dependence of a thing on that upon which it depends from one aspect; such as (A) depending on the existence of (B) and (B) depending on the existence of (A). It has types: first: concurrent circularity: example: the dependence of understanding fatherhood on understanding sonship and vice versa, and this type is not impossible because relations are mental matters that have no existence except in minds. Second: sequential circularity, which is the dependence of the existence of a thing on the existence of another thing which itself depends on the former; and this type is impossible because if each depended on the other, a thing would need itself, and a thing needing itself is impossible. And infinite regress is that the subject of cause and effect in a single chain from a particular effect goes on infinitely, such that the contingent depends in its existence on an effective cause, and that effective cause depends on another effective cause, and so on to infinity. The scholars of kalam have mentioned several proofs for the invalidity of infinite regress, including the proof of application and the proof of correlation. See: Tahrir al-Qawa'id al-Mantiqiyya by al-Qutb al-Razi p. 14, and Sharh al-Maqasid (2/111), and al-Qawl al-Sadid fi 'Ilm al-Tawhid by Shaykh Mahmud Abu Daqiqa (1/162-166), and al-Tahqiq al-Tamm fi 'Ilm al-Kalam by Muhammad al-Husayni al-Zawahiri p. 40. (2) Mukhtar al-Sihah, entry (fani). (3) Mukhtar al-Sihah, entry (bayda).
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage is a commentary explaining the attributes of God's pre-eternity (qidam) and everlastingness (baqa') from the Tahawiyyah creed. The commentator defines 'qadim' as essentially eternal without beginning, meaning that God's existence is not preceded by non-existence. If it were, that would necessitate either circularity (dawr) or infinite regress (tasalsul), both of which are impossible as established in kalam. He then refines the definition by noting that 'essential' (dhati) eternality excludes two other types: temporal eternality (qidam zamani), like yesterday relative to today, and relative eternality (qidam idafi), like a father relative to his son. Qidam is a negative attribute (sifa salbiyya) and is more specific than 'azal' (pre-eternity), because 'qadim' is an existent with no beginning, while 'azali' can apply to both existents (like God) and non-existents (like our pre-eternal non-existence). Next, he explains 'da'im' as everlasting without end, meaning that non-existence cannot happen to God's existence, because if something is proven to be eternal (qadim), its non-existence is impossible. Everlastingness (baqa') is also a negative attribute. The phrases 'la yafna' and 'la yabid' are confirmatory explanations: 'la yafna' means His remaining does not vanish, and 'la yabid' means His remaining is not cut off, citing lexical meanings from Mukhtar al-Sihah. The footnote, marked (1), provides a detailed explanation of the concepts of circularity (dawr) and infinite regress (tasalsul) used in the proof. It defines circularity as the dependence of a thing on what it itself depends on in one respect, giving the example of A needing B and B needing A. It distinguishes two types: concurrent circularity (dawr ma'i), which is not impossible because it involves mental relations (e.g., fatherhood and sonship), and sequential circularity (dawr sabqi), which is impossible because it would require a thing to depend on itself. Infinite regress (tasalsul) is defined as an endless chain of causes and effects, and the footnote notes that kalam scholars have provided proofs against it, such as the proof of application (burhan al-tatbiq) and the proof of correlation (burhan al-tadāyuf), citing several references. The entire passage serves to establish God's beginningless and endless existence as a rational necessity.

تسلسل
دور معي
دور سبقي
برهان التطبيق
برهان التضايف

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that circular reasoning is always invalid, but the passage shows that concurrent circularity (like fatherhood and sonship) is not impossible because it is mental.
Correction: The passage clarifies that only sequential circularity is impossible, while concurrent circularity is not.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which type of circularity is considered impossible?
A) Concurrent circularity
B) Sequential circularity
C) Both
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
Circularity (sequential) and infinite regress are logically impossible, necessitating a First Cause that is pre-eternal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the idea that sequential circularity is impossible because it would require a thing to depend on itself. Where in your own life do you find yourself relying on something that ultimately depends on you—like your reputation, your achievements, or your self-image? How does this logical truth expose the futility of such self-reliance?
?
If an infinite regress of causes is impossible, then your very existence points to a First Cause that has no cause. How does this demand that you live each day consciously dependent on that First Cause, rather than acting as if you are self-sufficient? What would change in your daily routine if you truly lived out this dependence?
?
The passage distinguishes between mental relations (like fatherhood and sonship) that can be circular without harm, and real existence that cannot. Are there areas where you confuse mental constructs—like your thoughts about yourself or others—with reality, causing you to treat imaginary dependencies as if they were real? How can this lesson free you from that confusion?

Proving Allah's Pre-Eternity: The Logic of Circularity and Infinite Regress
Maydani states the proof for Allah's pre-eternity via impossibility of circularity and infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof for Allah's pre-eternity (qidam) based on the impossibility of circularity (dawr) and infinite regress (tasalsul).

Core Idea
Allah's pre-eternity is rationally necessary to avoid circularity and infinite regress.

This passage lays the foundation for one of the most fundamental arguments in Islamic theology: the proof that Allah cannot have a beginning. If He had a beginning, His existence would be preceded by non-existence, leading to an impossible chain of dependencies. The author concisely states the argument, referring to the established impossibility of circularity and infinite regress.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic sentence and translation. Notice the structure: the creedal statement first, then the explanation of 'Qadim' with the conditional argument.
Pay attention to the phrase 'otherwise circularity or infinite regress would be necessary'.
2
Understand the conditional argument
Focus on the logical conditional: if Allah had a beginning (i.e., preceded by non-existence), then either circularity or infinite regress follows. Both are rationally impossible. Ask yourself why each is impossible.
The commentary will expand on these concepts.
3
Connect to broader theology
Reflect on why this rational proof is essential for establishing the Islamic concept of God as eternal without beginning. It shows that the existence of a being with no beginning is necessary to avoid absurdities.
