قَدِيمُ بِلَا ابْتِدَاءٍ دَائِمٌ بِلَا انْتِهَاءٍ، لَا يَفْنَى وَلَا يَبِيدُ.
(قديم) قدمًا ذاتيا (بلا ابتداء) أي ليس مسبوقاً بعدم وإلا لزم الدور أو التسلسل وكلاهما محال كما هو مقرر (١) .
وخرج بقيد الذاتي القدم الزماني كأمس بالنسبة لليوم والإضافي كالأب بالنسبة لولده، والقدم صفة سلبية أخص من الأزل لأن القديم موجود لا أول له، والأزلي ما لا أول له أعم من أن يكون وجوديًا كذات مولانا عز وجل أو عدميا كعدمنا الأزلي. (دائم) أي باقي (بلا انتهاء) أي ليس ملحوقًا بعدم؛ المعبر عنه بامتناع طروء العدم على وجوده تعالى؛ لأن من ثبت قدمه استحال عدمه، والبقاء صفة سلبية أيضًا، وقد أردفها على طريق التفسير والتأكيد بقوله (لا يفنى) أي لا يزول بقاؤه ، يقال : فنى الميت إذا زال وذهب أثره. مختار (۲).
(ولا يبيد) أي لا ينقطع بقاؤه ؛ يقال : بادت القبيلة؛ إذا انقطعت مختار (۳). (۱) الدور هو توقف الشيء على ما يتوقف عليه من جهة واحدة؛ كما يتوقف (أ) على حصول (ب) و (ب) على حصول (أ)، وله أنواع الأول: دور مَعِيٌّ: مثاله: توقف تعقل الأبوة على تعقل البنوة وبالعكس، وهذا القسم ليس بمستحيل؛ لأن الإضافات أمور اعتبارية لا وجود لها إلا في الأذهان الثاني: الدور السيقي وهو توقف وجود شيء على وجود شيء آخر قد توقف هذا الشيء الآخر عليه، وهذا القسم مستحيل لأنه لو توقف كل واحد منهما على الآخر لافتقر الشيء إلى نفسه وافتقار الشيء إلى نفسه محال. والتسلسل أن يتلاقى معروضا العلة والمعلولية في سلسلة واحدة من معلول معين إلى غير نهاية بأن يستند الممكن في وجوده إلى علة مؤثرة وتستند تلك العلة المؤثرة إلى علة مؤثرة وهلم جرا إلى غير نهاية، ولقد ذكر علماء الكلام عدة أدلة على بطلان التسلسل منها برهان التطبيق وبرهان التضايف
انظر: تحرير القواعد المنطقية للقطب الرازي ص (١٤)، وشرح المقاصد (۲/ ۱۱۱)، والقول السديد في علم التوحيد للشيخ محمود أبو دقيقة (١٦٢/١-١٦٦)، والتحقيق التام في علم الكلام لمحمد الحسيني الظواهري ص (٤٠).
(۲) مختار الصحاح مادة (فني).
(۳) مختار الصحاح، مادة (بيد).
He is Qadim (pre-eternal) without beginning, Da'im (everlasting) without end, He does not perish nor cease to exist. (Qadim) means self-subsisting pre-eternity (qidam dhati) (without beginning) i.e., not preceded by non-existence, otherwise circularity (al-dawr) or infinite regress (al-tasalsul) would be necessary, and both are impossible as is established (1). And by the restriction of self-subsisting (al-dhati) is excluded temporal pre-eternity (al-qidam al-zamani) like yesterday relative to today, and relative pre-eternity like the father relative to his son. And pre-eternity (al-qidam) is a negative attribute, more specific than al-azal (eternity without beginning) because al-qadim is an existent with no beginning, and al-azali is that which has no beginning, more general, whether existential like the Essence of our Lord, may He be exalted, or non-existential like our non-existence in pre-eternity. (Da'im) i.e., everlasting (baqi) (without end) i.e., not followed by non-existence; expressed as the impossibility of non-existence befalling His existence, may He be exalted; because whoever's pre-eternity is established, his non-existence is impossible. And al-baqa' (everlastingness) is also a negative attribute, and He appended it by way of interpretation and confirmation with His saying (la yafna) i.e., His everlastingness does not vanish; it is said: faniya al-mayyitu if it vanished and its effect disappeared. (Mukhtar) (2). (Wa la yabid) i.e., His everlastingness does not cease; it is said: badat al-qabilatu if it became extinct. (Mukhtar) (3). (1) Al-dawr (circularity) is the dependence of a thing on that which it depends on, from one direction; as (A) depends on the occurrence of (B) and (B) depends on the occurrence of (A). It has types: First: concurrent circularity (al-dawr al-ma'i): example: the dependence of the conceptualization of fatherhood on the conceptualization of sonship and vice versa. This type is not impossible because relations (al-idafat) are mental considerations having no existence except in minds. Second: sequential circularity (al-dawr al-sabqi): it is the dependence of the existence of a thing on the existence of another thing which itself depends on that other thing. This type is impossible because if each depends on the other, the thing would depend on itself, and self-dependence is impossible. And al-tasalsul (infinite regress) is that the relationship of cause and effect is actualized in a single chain from a specific effect to infinity, i.e., the contingent depends in its existence on an effective cause, and that effective cause depends on an effective cause, and so on to infinity. And the scholars of kalam have mentioned several proofs for the invalidity of infinite regress, including the proof of application (burhan al-tatbiq) and the proof of mutual relation (burhan al-tadāyuf). See: Tahreer al-Qawa'id al-Mantiqiyya by al-Qutb al-Razi p. (14), and Sharh al-Maqasid (2/111), and al-Qawl al-Sadeed fi 'Ilm al-Tawheed by Shaykh Mahmoud Abu Daqiqa (1/162-166), and al-Tahqeeq al-Tamm fi 'Ilm al-Kalam by Muhammad al-Husayni al-Zawahiri p. (40). (2) Mukhtar al-Sihah, entry (faniya). (3) Mukhtar al-Sihah, entry (bada).
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage is a definition and explanation of the divine attributes of pre-eternity (qidam) and everlastingness (baqa'). The author begins by quoting the creedal statement that Allah is Qadim (pre-eternal) without beginning and Da'im (everlasting) without end, and that He does not perish (la yafna) nor cease (la yabid). He then explains each term. First, 'Qadim' means self-subsisting pre-eternity, not preceded by non-existence. He argues that if it were preceded by non-existence, circularity (dawr) or infinite regress (tasalsul) would result, both of which are impossible. He distinguishes self-subsisting pre-eternity from temporal pre-eternity (like 'yesterday' relative to 'today') and relative pre-eternity (like a father relative to his son). Pre-eternity is a negative attribute. He contrasts it with al-azal, which is broader: al-azali can apply to both existents and non-existents, whereas al-qadim applies only to an existent with no beginning. Next, 'Da'im' means everlasting, not followed by non-existence. He states that if something is proven to be pre-eternal, its non-existence becomes impossible. Everlastingness (baqa') is also a negative attribute. He explains that 'la yafna' and 'la yabid' are appended for emphasis, both meaning that His everlastingness does not disappear or cease. The footnote provides a detailed technical explanation of circularity and infinite regress. It defines circularity as the dependence of a thing on that which it depends on, and identifies two types: concurrent circularity (in mental relations, which is possible) and sequential circularity (in existence, which is impossible because it leads to self-dependence). Infinite regress is defined as an endless chain of causes and effects. The footnote mentions that kalam scholars have adduced proofs against it, such as the proof of application (burhan al-tatbiq) and proof of mutual relation (burhan al-tadāyuf), with references.

الدور (al-dawr)
التسلسل (al-tasalsul)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that 'Qadim' simply means 'very old' or 'ancient', similar to created things that have a beginning in time.
Correction: In kalam, 'Qadim' means having no beginning at all, not merely being old. It is a negative attribute that negates the possibility of a beginning.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what would be necessary if Allah were preceded by non-existence?
A) Temporal pre-eternity
B) Circularity or infinite regress
C) Relativity
D) A negative attribute

Key Takeaway
The proof that Allah is Qadim rests on the rational impossibility of circularity and infinite regress, showing that His existence must have no beginning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Allah had a beginning, He would depend on something else. Do you truly live as though you depend on the One who depends on nothing? In what areas of your life today will you consciously rely on the Eternal (al-Qadim) instead of fleeting things?
?
The proof shows that accepting a beginning for Allah leads to absurdities. Have you ever considered that your own doubts about Allah's attributes might stem from not following the logic through? Will you commit to studying the rational proofs so that your certainty in Allah's perfection is solid?
?
You believe Allah is pre-eternal, but does your heart feel the weight of that? If He has always existed and always will, what truly matters in your short life? What will you change today to align your priorities with the Eternal rather than the temporary?

Distinguishing True Pre-Eternity: Self-Subsisting vs. Temporal and Relative
Maydani distinguishes types of pre-eternity and defines qidam as a negative attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between self-subsisting pre-eternity (al-qidam al-dhati) and other types of pre-eternity, and why Allah's pre-eternity is a negative attribute.

Core Idea
Allah's pre-eternity is self-subsisting, not temporal or relative, and is a negative attribute more specific than al-azal.

After establishing the proof of Allah's pre-eternity, the author clarifies what kind of pre-eternity is intended. He distinguishes self-subsisting pre-eternity from two other types: temporal pre-eternity (like yesterday relative to today) and relative pre-eternity (like a father relative to his son). Then he explains that qidam is a negative attribute and compares it with the broader term azal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types
Read the passage and list the three types of pre-eternity mentioned: self-subsisting, temporal, and relative. Understand why the latter two are not applicable to Allah.
Temporal pre-eternity is relative to another point in time.
2
Understand negative attribute
Focus on the statement that qidam is a negative attribute. What does it negate? It negates a beginning. Also note how qidam is more specific than azal.
Al-azal can apply to non-existents, but qidam only to existents.
3
Apply the distinction
Reflect on how this distinction prevents misunderstandings about Allah's eternity—e.g., that His pre-eternity is not like that of created things that are merely old.

(ولا يبيد) أي لا ينقطع بقاؤه ؛ يقال : بادت القبيلة؛ إذا انقطعت مختار (۳). (۱) الدور هو توقف الشيء على ما يتوقف عليه من جهة واحدة؛ كما يتوقف (أ) على حصول (ب) و (ب) على حصول (أ)، وله أنواع الأول: دور مَعِيٌّ: مثاله: توقف تعقل الأبوة على تعقل البنوة وبالعكس، وهذا القسم ليس بمستحيل؛ لأن الإضافات أمور اعتبارية لا وجود لها إلا في الأذهان الثاني: الدور السيقي وهو توقف وجود شيء على وجود شيء آخر قد توقف هذا الشيء الآخر عليه، وهذا القسم مستحيل لأنه لو توقف كل واحد منهما على الآخر لافتقر الشيء إلى نفسه وافتقار الشيء إلى نفسه محال. والتسلسل أن يتلاقى معروضا العلة والمعلولية في سلسلة واحدة من معلول معين إلى غير نهاية بأن يستند الممكن في وجوده إلى علة مؤثرة وتستند تلك العلة المؤثرة إلى علة مؤثرة وهلم جرا إلى غير نهاية، ولقد ذكر علماء الكلام عدة أدلة على بطلان التسلسل منها برهان التطبيق وبرهان التضايف
انظر: تحرير القواعد المنطقية للقطب الرازي ص (١٤)، وشرح المقاصد (۲/ ۱۱۱)، والقول السديد في علم التوحيد للشيخ محمود أبو دقيقة (١٦٢/١-١٦٦)، والتحقيق التام في علم الكلام لمحمد الحسيني الظواهري ص (٤٠).
(۲) مختار الصحاح مادة (فني).
(۳) مختار الصحاح، مادة (بيد).
He is Qadim (pre-eternal) without beginning, Da'im (everlasting) without end, He does not perish nor cease to exist. (Qadim) means self-subsisting pre-eternity (qidam dhati) (without beginning) i.e., not preceded by non-existence, otherwise circularity (al-dawr) or infinite regress (al-tasalsul) would be necessary, and both are impossible as is established (1). And by the restriction of self-subsisting (al-dhati) is excluded temporal pre-eternity (al-qidam al-zamani) like yesterday relative to today, and relative pre-eternity like the father relative to his son. And pre-eternity (al-qidam) is a negative attribute, more specific than al-azal (eternity without beginning) because al-qadim is an existent with no beginning, and al-azali is that which has no beginning, more general, whether existential like the Essence of our Lord, may He be exalted, or non-existential like our non-existence in pre-eternity. (Da'im) i.e., everlasting (baqi) (without end) i.e., not followed by non-existence; expressed as the impossibility of non-existence befalling His existence, may He be exalted; because whoever's pre-eternity is established, his non-existence is impossible. And al-baqa' (everlastingness) is also a negative attribute, and He appended it by way of interpretation and confirmation with His saying (la yafna) i.e., His everlastingness does not vanish; it is said: faniya al-mayyitu if it vanished and its effect disappeared. (Mukhtar) (2). (Wa la yabid) i.e., His everlastingness does not cease; it is said: badat al-qabilatu if it became extinct. (Mukhtar) (3). (1) Al-dawr (circularity) is the dependence of a thing on that which it depends on, from one direction; as (A) depends on the occurrence of (B) and (B) depends on the occurrence of (A). It has types: First: concurrent circularity (al-dawr al-ma'i): example: the dependence of the conceptualization of fatherhood on the conceptualization of sonship and vice versa. This type is not impossible because relations (al-idafat) are mental considerations having no existence except in minds. Second: sequential circularity (al-dawr al-sabqi): it is the dependence of the existence of a thing on the existence of another thing which itself depends on that other thing. This type is impossible because if each depends on the other, the thing would depend on itself, and self-dependence is impossible. And al-tasalsul (infinite regress) is that the relationship of cause and effect is actualized in a single chain from a specific effect to infinity, i.e., the contingent depends in its existence on an effective cause, and that effective cause depends on an effective cause, and so on to infinity. And the scholars of kalam have mentioned several proofs for the invalidity of infinite regress, including the proof of application (burhan al-tatbiq) and the proof of mutual relation (burhan al-tadāyuf). See: Tahreer al-Qawa'id al-Mantiqiyya by al-Qutb al-Razi p. (14), and Sharh al-Maqasid (2/111), and al-Qawl al-Sadeed fi 'Ilm al-Tawheed by Shaykh Mahmoud Abu Daqiqa (1/162-166), and al-Tahqeeq al-Tamm fi 'Ilm al-Kalam by Muhammad al-Husayni al-Zawahiri p. (40). (2) Mukhtar al-Sihah, entry (faniya). (3) Mukhtar al-Sihah, entry (bada).
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage is a definition and explanation of the divine attributes of pre-eternity (qidam) and everlastingness (baqa'). The author begins by quoting the creedal statement that Allah is Qadim (pre-eternal) without beginning and Da'im (everlasting) without end, and that He does not perish (la yafna) nor cease (la yabid). He then explains each term. First, 'Qadim' means self-subsisting pre-eternity, not preceded by non-existence. He argues that if it were preceded by non-existence, circularity (dawr) or infinite regress (tasalsul) would result, both of which are impossible. He distinguishes self-subsisting pre-eternity from temporal pre-eternity (like 'yesterday' relative to 'today') and relative pre-eternity (like a father relative to his son). Pre-eternity is a negative attribute. He contrasts it with al-azal, which is broader: al-azali can apply to both existents and non-existents, whereas al-qadim applies only to an existent with no beginning. Next, 'Da'im' means everlasting, not followed by non-existence. He states that if something is proven to be pre-eternal, its non-existence becomes impossible. Everlastingness (baqa') is also a negative attribute. He explains that 'la yafna' and 'la yabid' are appended for emphasis, both meaning that His everlastingness does not disappear or cease. The footnote provides a detailed technical explanation of circularity and infinite regress. It defines circularity as the dependence of a thing on that which it depends on, and identifies two types: concurrent circularity (in mental relations, which is possible) and sequential circularity (in existence, which is impossible because it leads to self-dependence). Infinite regress is defined as an endless chain of causes and effects. The footnote mentions that kalam scholars have adduced proofs against it, such as the proof of application (burhan al-tatbiq) and proof of mutual relation (burhan al-tadāyuf), with references.

Key Terms
القدم الذاتي (al-qidam al-dhati)
القدم الزماني (al-qidam al-zamani)
القدم الإضافي (al-qidam al-idafi)
صفة سلبية (sifah salbiyyah)
الأزل (al-azal)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that 'Qadim' can be applied to anything that is old, like mountains or the universe.
Correction: In kalam, 'Qadim' (pre-eternal) is a technical term meaning having no beginning at all, not simply being old. The created world is not Qadim because it has a beginning.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is pre-eternity (qidam) more specific than al-azal?
A) Because qidam only applies to existents with no beginning
B) Because qidam is a positive attribute
C) Because azal is only used for Allah
D) Because qidam includes non-existents

Key Takeaway
Allah's pre-eternity is self-subsisting, not relative, and is a negative attribute that is more specific than the broader term al-azal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have learned that Allah's pre-eternity is self-subsisting, not relative like the pre-eternity of a father to his son. When you consider your own existence, which began at a specific moment, how does the absolute beginninglessness of Allah change the way you view your own life's span and your dependence on Him? What would it mean to live each day with the awareness that you are contingent and He alone is necessary?
?
The passage describes qidam as a 'negative attribute' — it does not tell us what Allah is, but what He is not: He has no beginning. Have you ever caught yourself imagining Allah's eternity as if it were an extended timeline, like your own but longer? How can reminding yourself that His eternity is absolutely without beginning reshape your worship and trust in His promises?
?
If al-azal can apply even to non-existents (like your non-existence before you were born), while qidam only applies to existent beings, what does this tell you about the uniqueness of Allah's existence? Are you willing to let go of any trace of assuming that your own existence is somehow self-sufficient? What concrete step can you take today to acknowledge your absolute dependence on the Self-Subsisting One?

Divine Attributes

Understanding Allah's Everlastingness (Baqa') as a Negative Attribute
Maydani defines everlastingness as a negative attribute and explains its emphasis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the attribute of everlastingness (baqa'), its rational connection to pre-eternity, and its emphasis through the terms 'la yafna' and 'la yabid'.

Core Idea
Because Allah is pre-eternal, His non-existence is impossible, and everlastingness is a negative attribute emphasized by two synonymous terms.

The passage now turns to the second attribute: Da'im (everlasting) or Baqa'. The author explains that since pre-eternity is established, everlastingness logically follows – if something has no beginning, it cannot have an end. He then emphasizes this by explaining the two appended phrases 'la yafna' and 'la yabid', both meaning that His existence does not cease or perish.

Guided Path
1
Understand the logical link
Read the passage and note the phrase 'because whoever's pre-eternity is established, his non-existence is impossible'. This is a key logical connection between qadim and da'im.
This shows that everlastingness is not an independent attribute but a consequence.
2
Identify the negative attribute
Realize that baqa' is also a negative attribute – it negates the possibility of non-existence. Also note that 'la yafna' and 'la yabid' are explained as emphasis and interpretation.
The author uses lexical definitions to reinforce the meaning.
3
Reflect on linguistic nuances
Look at the lexical explanations from Mukhtar al-Sihah: 'faniya' means to vanish, 'badat' means to become extinct. This emphasizes that Allah's existence does not cease in any way.

قَدِيمُ بِلَا ابْتِدَاءٍ دَائِمٌ بِلَا انْتِهَاءٍ، لَا يَفْنَى وَلَا يَبِيدُ.
(قديم) قدمًا ذاتيا (بلا ابتداء) أي ليس مسبوقاً بعدم وإلا لزم الدور أو التسلسل وكلاهما محال كما هو مقرر (١) .
وخرج بقيد الذاتي القدم الزماني كأمس بالنسبة لليوم والإضافي كالأب بالنسبة لولده، والقدم صفة سلبية أخص من الأزل لأن القديم موجود لا أول له، والأزلي ما لا أول له أعم من أن يكون وجوديًا كذات مولانا عز وجل أو عدميا كعدمنا الأزلي. (دائم) أي باقي (بلا انتهاء) أي ليس ملحوقًا بعدم؛ المعبر عنه بامتناع طروء العدم على وجوده تعالى؛ لأن من ثبت قدمه استحال عدمه، والبقاء صفة سلبية أيضًا، وقد أردفها على طريق التفسير والتأكيد بقوله (لا يفنى) أي لا يزول بقاؤه ، يقال : فنى الميت إذا زال وذهب أثره. مختار (۲).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that everlastingness (baqa') is an independent attribute that could be true of something that is not pre-eternal, like the soul or the world after creation.
Correction: In kalam, true everlastingness that is necessary belongs only to Allah and is logically tied to pre-eternity: if something has a beginning, it is possible for it to cease, so only what is pre-eternal can be necessarily everlasting.

A) Because He is all-powerful
B) Because His pre-eternity has been established

Allah's everlastingness (baqa') is a necessary consequence of His pre-eternity and is a negative attribute confirmed by the emphatic terms 'la yafna' and 'la yabid'.

?

What concrete step can you take today to reduce your fear of death or loss?

Do you treat this fact as a comforting intellectual belief, or does it transform how you face your own mortality and the temporary nature of everything else? The Eternal Attribute of Divine Will
Maydani defines divine will and summarizes theological disagreements.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of Allah's will as an eternal attribute and the key theological disagreements about its nature.

Core Idea
Allah's will is eternal, yet its nature is debated among sects.

This passage from Maydani's commentary establishes the Sunni definition of divine will while also surveying how other theological schools understand it. You'll see that despite universal agreement that Allah is 'willing', the meaning of that will is contested. The text presents four distinct positions, helping you navigate a key point of kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the creedal statement
Start with the statement 'And there is nothing that occurs except what He wills.' Reflect on its comprehensive meaning: nothing happens outside Allah's will.
This is the foundation of Sunni belief in divine decree.
2
Study the definition of irada
Maydani defines irada as an eternal attribute that specifies contingent things. Note the key terms: 'eternal attribute,' 'subsisting in His Essence,' and 'specifies.'
3
Examine the universal agreement
Al-Laqani points out that all groups agree Allah is willing. This sets the stage for the disagreements that follow—they are about modality, not existence.
4
Review the four positions
Go through each of the four views: al-Jubba'iyya, al-Karramiyya, Dirar, and the verifiers of the Mu'tazila. Understand how each differs from the Sunni view.
Use the guided breakdown below to compare them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَلَا يَكُونُ إِلَّا مَا يُرِيدُ.
(ولا يكون) أي لا يوجد في ملكه إلا ما يشاء و (يريد)، والإرادة: صفة أزلية قائمة بذاته تعالى تخصص كل ممكن ببعض ما يجوز عليه؛ قال اللقاني: واعلم أن الخلاف في معنى إرادته تعالى كثير والقول في تفصيله شهير؛ مع اتفاق المتكلمين والحكماء وجميع الفرق على القول بأنه تعالى مريد؛ فعند الجبائية (٢): صفة زائدة قائمة لا بمحل، وعند الكرَّامية (۳): صفة حادثة قائمة بالذات، وعند ضرار (1) نفس الذات، وعند محققي المعتزلة هي العلم بما في الفعل من [?].
(۱) إبراهيم بن إبراهيم بن حسن برهان الدين أبو الأمداد المصري المالكي المعروف باللقاني نسبة إلى لقانة من قرى مصر؛ من مشاهير علماء الكلام، أخذ عن الرملي شارح المنهاج وابن قاسم، وغيرهم، ومن تلاميذه ولده عبد السلام والعلاء الشَّبراملسي ومحمد الخرشي المالكي وغيرهم، ومن تصانيفه: منظومة جوهرة التوحيد وشرحها، وقضاء الوطر من نزهة النظر، وغير ذلك من المؤلفات، توفي سنة ١٠٤١هـ. انظر ترجمته في معجم المؤلفين (۲/۱) .
(۲) هم أتباع أبي علي محمد بن عبد الوهاب الجبائي، وهم طائفة من معتزلة البصرة انفردوا عن أصحابهم بمسائل؛ منها ما اتفقوا فيه مع البهشمية من أتباع أبي هاشم بن أبي علي الجبائي كالقول بموجودات لا محل لها، ومنها ما انفردوا به عنهم كقولهم بأن الله عالم لذاته قادر حي لذاته أي لا يقتضي كونه عالما صفة هي علم أو حال توجب كونه عالما، وغير ذلك.
انظر: الفرق بين الفرق ص ۱۱۰-۱۱۱) ، الملل والنحل للشهرستاني (٧٨/١-٨٥). (۳) هم أتباع أبي عبد الله محمد بن كرام، وكان من زهاد سجستان، وهم فرق كثيرة، وكلهم يعتقدون أن الله تعالى جسم وجوهر ومحل للحوادث ويثبتون له جهة ومكانًا.
انظر الفرق بين الفرق ص (۱۳۰-۱۳۷)، اعتقادات فرق المسلمين والمشركين ص (۱۰۱-۱۰۲).
(٤) ضرار بن عمرو، من رءوس المعتزلة، وشيخ الضرارية منهم، كان في بده أمره تلميذا لواصل بن عطاء، ثم خالفه في خلق الأعمال فوافق أهل السنة فيها، ومن مقولته: أن الاستطاعة قبل الفعل - وقد وافق القدرية في ذلك وأن الله تعالى يُرى في الآخرة بحاسة سادسة.
انظر: سير أعلام النبلاء (٥٤٤/١٠) ، الفرق بين الفرق ص (۱۲۹-۱۳۰)، اعتقادات فرق
And there is nothing that occurs except what He wills. (And there is nothing that occurs) i.e., nothing exists in His kingdom except what He wills and desires. And al-irada (will) is an eternal attribute subsisting in His Essence, may He be exalted, that specifies every contingent thing with some of its possible aspects. Al-Laqani said: 'Know that the disagreement concerning the meaning of His will, exalted is He, is extensive, and the sayings detailing it are well-known, despite the agreement of the mutakallimun, the philosophers, and all sects that He, exalted is He, is willing. For according to al-Jubba'iyya (2): it is an additional attribute subsisting not in a locus. According to al-Karramiyya (3): it is an originated attribute subsisting in the Essence. According to Dirar (1): it is the Essence itself. According to the verifiers of the Mu'tazila: it is knowledge of the benefit in the act [?].' (1) Ibrahim bin Ibrahim bin Hasan Burhan al-Din Abu al-Imdad al-Misri al-Maliki known as al-Laqani, from the village of Laqana in Egypt; one of the prominent scholars of kalam, he studied under al-Ramli, the commentator of al-Minhaj, and Ibn Qasim, and others. Among his students are his son Abd al-Salam, al-'Ala' al-Shabramallisi, Muhammad al-Khurashi al-Maliki, and others. Among his works are the poem Jawharat al-Tawhid and its commentary, and Qada' al-Watar min Nuzhat al-Nadhar, and other writings. He died in 1041 AH. See his biography in Mu'jam al-Mu'allifin (2/1). (2) They are the followers of Abu Ali Muhammad bin Abd al-Wahhab al-Jubba'i, a sect of the Mu'tazila of Basra. They differed from their associates on several issues, including agreement with the Bahshamiyya on the existence of things with no locus, and their own view that God is knowing by His essence, powerful by His essence, living by His essence—i.e., His being knowing does not necessitate an attribute of knowledge or a state that makes Him knowing, etc. See: al-Farq bayn al-Firaq p. 110-111, al-Milal wa al-Nihal by al-Shahrastani (1/78-85). (3) They are the followers of Abu Abd Allah Muhammad bin Karram, from the ascetics of Sijistan. They consist of many sects, all believing that God is a body, a substance, and a locus for events, and they affirm direction and place for Him. See: al-Farq bayn al-Firaq p. 130-137, I'tiqadat Firaq al-Muslimin wa al-Mushrikin p. 101-102. (4) Dirar bin Amr, one of the leaders of the Mu'tazila, and the shaykh of the Dirariyya among them. In the beginning of his career he was a student of Wasil bin Ata', then he disagreed with him on the creation of actions and agreed with Ahl al-Sunna on that. Among his statements: that ability (al-istita'a) precedes the action—and he agreed with the Qadariyya in that—and that God will be seen in the afterlife with a sixth sense. See: Siyar A'lam al-Nubala' (10/544), al-Farq bayn al-Firaq p. 129-130, I'tiqadat Firaq.
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage is a definition and theological survey of the divine attribute of will (irada). It begins with the creedal statement 'And there is nothing that occurs except what He wills,' which the commentator explains as meaning that nothing in Allah's kingdom occurs except by His will and desire. He then defines irada as an eternal attribute subsisting in Allah's Essence that specifies every contingent thing with certain of its possible aspects—i.e., it determines which possibility becomes actual. The author then quotes al-Laqani, who notes that despite universal agreement among mutakallimun, philosophers, and all sects that Allah is willing, there is extensive disagreement on the precise meaning and nature of His will. Al-Laqani summarizes four positions: (1) The Jubba'iyya (a Mu'tazili subgroup) say it is an additional attribute that subsists not in a locus—they deny that it subsists in Allah's Essence. (2) The Karramiyya say it is an originated (haditha) attribute that subsists in the Essence—they believe it is temporal. (3) Dirar bin Amr says it is identical to the Essence itself. (4) The verifiers (muhaqqiqun) of the Mu'tazila say it is knowledge of the benefit in the act (though the text cuts off). The footnote then provides biographical information about al-Laqani and descriptions of each mentioned sect: the Jubba'iyya, the Karramiyya, and Dirar and his followers.

صفة أزلية
الجبائية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that because there is disagreement about the nature of divine will, it means God does not have a will at all.
Correction: The text explicitly states that all groups—mutakallimun, philosophers, and all sects—agree that Allah is willing (mureed). The disagreement is only about how to understand that will.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which group holds that Allah's will is an originated (haditha) attribute subsisting in the Essence?
A) The Jubba'iyya
B) The Karramiyya
C) Dirar bin Amr
D) The verifiers of the Mu'tazila

Key Takeaway
Allah's will is an eternal attribute that determines all contingent things, and while all sects affirm He is willing, they differ sharply on its nature.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of one recent event in your life that you disliked or found difficult. Do you truly believe it occurred only because Allah willed it? How does that belief change the way you respond to that event today? Commit to saying 'Qaddar Allah wa ma sha'a fa'ala' (Allah decreed and what He wills He does) when you feel resentment.
?
The passage shows that even scholars disagreed about the nature of Allah's will, yet all affirmed He is willing. Is your own certainty in Allah's will stronger than your intellectual curiosity about how it works? Right now, where do you need to submit to His will without demanding explanations? Write one area of your life where you will actively surrender your plans to His will this week.
?
The attribute of will is eternal—it never changes. How does this confront your tendency to assume that Allah's will might 'adapt' to your desires or circumstances? What would it cost you to live today fully trusting that whatever happens, it is precisely what He willed for you from eternity?

The Divine Will: Definition, Sectarian Views, and the Ahl al-Sunna Position
Defines al-irada, enumerates five sectarian views, and states the Ahl al-Sunna position

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ahl al-Sunna concept of divine will as an eternal attribute of specification, and how it differs from other sectarian views.

Core Idea
Divine will is the attribute of specification that cannot be reduced to knowledge or essence.

This lesson examines the nature of Allah's will (al-irada). While all Muslims affirm that Allah wills, there is deep disagreement on what exactly that means. The passage from the Sharh al-Tahawiyyah presents the Ahl al-Sunna position against five other views, grounding it in the logical necessity of a specifying attribute.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the flow: definition, mention of disagreement, enumeration of views, statement of truth.
The passage begins with a gloss of the creedal phrase.
2
Identify the definition of al-irada
The text gives a clear definition: an eternal attribute that specifies each possible thing. Understand the term 'specification' (takhsis).
This is the core of the Ahl al-Sunna view.
3
List the five deviant views
The passage enumerates five alternative views from different sects. Write them down and note how each differs from the correct view.
Contrast each with the Ahl al-Sunna concept of an additional eternal attribute.
4
Study the rational proof
The passage provides a brief argument: specification of some possibilities over others requires a specifying attribute; it cannot be due to essence alone or external factors. Reflect on why this is necessary.
This is a key kalam argument for the reality of the attribute of will.
5
Review the key terms and take the quiz
Ensure you understand the Arabic terms for the sects and concepts. Then take the checkpoint quiz to test your comprehension.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَلَا يَكُونُ إِلَّا مَا يُرِيدُ. (ولا يكون) أي لا يوجد في ملكه إلا ما يشاء و (يريد)، والإرادة: صفة أزلية قائمة بذاته تعالى تخصص كل ممكن ببعض ما يجوز عليه؛ قال اللقاني: واعلم أن الخلاف في معنى إرادته تعالى كثير والقول في تفصيله شهير؛ مع اتفاق المتكلمين والحكماء وجميع الفرق على القول بأنه تعالى مريد؛ فعند الجبائية: صفة زائدة قائمة لا بمحل، وعند الكرَّامية: صفة حادثة قائمة بالذات، وعند ضرار: نفس الذات، وعند محققي المعتزلة هي العلم بما في الفعل من المصلحة، وعند الحكماء والفلاسفة: هي العلم بالنظام الأكمل. والحق عندنا - كما قاله السعد: إنها صفة شأنها التخصيص قديمة زائدة قائمة به على ما هو شأن سائر الصفات الحقيقية؛ لأن تخصيص بعض الأضداد بالوقوع دون البعض وفي بعض الأوقات دون البعض مع استواء نسبة الذات إلى الكل لابد أن يكون لصفة شأنها التخصيص؛ لامتناع التخصيص بلا مخصص وامتناع احتياج الواجب في فاعليته إلى أمر منفصل. انتهى.
And nothing occurs except what He wills. (Nothing occurs) meaning nothing exists in His dominion except what He wishes and wills. And al-irada (will) is an eternal attribute subsisting in His essence, may He be exalted, that specifies every possible thing with some of what is permissible for it. Al-Luqani said: Know that the disagreement concerning the meaning of His will is extensive and the saying in its detail is well-known, despite the agreement of the mutakallimun (theologians), the hukama' (philosophers), and all sects upon the statement that He, exalted, is willing (murid). According to al-Jubba'iyya: it is an additional attribute subsisting not in a locus. According to al-Karramiyya: it is a contingent (haditha) attribute subsisting in the essence. According to Dirar: it is the essence itself. According to the verifying scholars of the Mu'tazila: it is knowledge of what is in the act of benefit (maslaha). According to the hukama' and philosophers: it is knowledge of the most perfect system (al-nizam al-akmal). And the truth according to us — as al-Sa'd (al-Taftazani) said: it is an attribute whose function is specification, eternal, additional, subsisting in Him, as is the case with the rest of the real attributes; because the specification of some opposites to occur rather than others and at some times rather than others, despite the equal relation of the essence to all, must be due to an attribute whose function is specification; due to the impossibility of specification without a specifier (mukhassis) and the impossibility of the Necessary being in need, in His effecting (fa'iliyya), of something separate. End.
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage defines divine will (al-irada) and presents the correct Ahl al-Sunna doctrine against various sectarian views. It begins by glossing the phrase 'And nothing occurs except what He wills' from the Tahawiyyah creed, explaining that nothing exists in Allah's dominion except what He wishes and wills. The commentator then provides a technical definition: al-irada is an eternal attribute subsisting in Allah's essence that specifies each possible thing with particular permissible qualities or states. This specification function distinguishes irada from other attributes. Al-Luqani is then quoted to set up the discussion: despite universal agreement that Allah is willing, there is extensive disagreement on the nature of that will. The passage enumerates five deviant views: (1) al-Jubba'iyya (a Mu'tazili subsect) says it is an additional attribute not subsisting in a locus; (2) al-Karramiyya (anthropomorphists) say it is a contingent attribute subsisting in the essence; (3) Dirar ibn 'Amr says it is identical to the essence; (4) the verifying Mu'tazila say it is knowledge of what is beneficial in the act; (5) the philosophers say it is knowledge of the most perfect system. Then the correct view is given, citing Sa'd al-Din al-Taftazani: irada is an eternal, additional attribute whose very function is specification. The reasoning is that given the equal relation of the essence to all possibilities, the specification of some opposites to occur rather than others, and at particular times, cannot happen without a specifying attribute. To deny this would require either specification without a specifier (impossible) or the Necessary being's effecting requiring something external (also impossible). Thus irada is a real, eternal attribute of specification.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Divine will is merely knowledge of what is best or most beneficial.
Correction: Ahl al-Sunna holds that divine will is a distinct attribute of specification that goes beyond knowledge. It is the active determination of which possibilities become actual.

Checkpoint
According to the Ahl al-Sunna view presented in this passage, what is the function (shān) of the attribute of divine will (al-irāda)?
A) Knowledge of the most perfect system
B) Bringing benefits into existence
C) Specification of some possibilities over others
D) Being identical to the divine essence

Key Takeaway
Divine will is an eternal, additional attribute of specification distinct from knowledge and essence, necessary to explain why some possibilities occur rather than others.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that everything that happens in your life—even the trials and disappointments—occurs only by Allah's will? When something bad happens, do you secretly think it is chance or unfair, or do you submit to His specification? Test yourself: can you say 'It is from Allah' without bitterness?
?
If divine will is an eternal attribute that specifies every detail, then what place do you have for regret or anxiety about the past? How can you train your heart to accept that every moment was precisely chosen by Him, even those you wish had been different? Will you today forgive yourself and others, knowing that nothing escapes His will?
?
The passage shows that some sects reduced Allah's will to mere knowledge or to His essence. Have you ever reduced your understanding of Allah's will to just 'He knows what is best' without truly affirming that He actively and specifically chooses your circumstances? How can you deepen your worship of Him as al-Murid (the Willer), not just al-'Alim (the All-Knowing)?

Irada and Mashi'a: One in Allah, Distinct in Humans
First caution on irada vs mashi'a with legal example and scholar's critique.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the theological and legal distinction between irada and mashi'a in the context of divine and human agency.

Core Idea
In Allah, will is one; in humans, mere intention differs from effective volition.

The first caution clarifies a subtle but important point about the divine attribute of will. While the terms irada and mashi'a are synonymous for Allah, they carry different weight in human affairs. This distinction has practical legal implications, as illustrated by the example of divorce. Let's read how the scholars explain this.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the full Arabic text of the first caution once. Note the flow: statement of unity in Allah, then distinction in humans, then illustration, then etymology, then al-Qunawi's critique and resolution.
Focus on the derivation and the legal example.
2
Analyze the legal example
Examine the example: a husband says 'I intended divorce' vs. 'I wished divorce.' Why does one not effect divorce while the other does? The passage gives linguistic reasons.
Relate to the concepts of hesitation and bringing into existence.
3
Understand al-Qunawi's critique
Al-Qunawi objects that if mashi'a simply meant 'bringing into existence', intention (niyya) would be unnecessary. See how he refines the distinction: mashi'a is complete irada that is inevitably followed by action.
This leads to the conclusion about complete vs. incomplete will.
4
Apply to divine and human contexts
Connect the refined distinction to Allah and humans. Allah's will is always complete and effective; human will may be mere intent without action.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تنبیهان الأول: الإرادة والمشيئة واحدة عندنا في حق الله تعالى؛ أما في جانب العباد فيفترقان؛ حتى لو قال لامرأته: أردت طلاقك لا تطلق، ولو قال شئت طلاقك يقع؛ لأن الإرادة مشتقة من التردد وهو الطلب والمشيئة عبارة عن الإيجاد فكأنه قال: أوجدت طلاقك، وبه يقع الطلاق، كذا ذكروه. قال القونوي: وفيه نظر؛ إذ لو كان كذلك لما احتيج إلى النية، والحاصل أن المشيئة عبارة عن الإرادة التامة التي لا يتخلف عنها الفعل، والإرادة تطلق على التامة وعلى غير التامة؛ فالأولى هي المرادة في جانب الله تعالى، والثانية في جانب العباد. اهـ. الثاني: قال اللقاني: مذهب أهل الحق أن كل ما أراده الله تعالى فهو كائن وكل
Two cautions. First: Al-irada and al-mashi'a are one [in meaning] according to us with respect to Allah, exalted; but with respect to servants they differ; such that if a man says to his wife 'I intended (aradtu) your divorce,' she is not divorced, but if he says 'I wished (shi'tu) your divorce,' it occurs; because al-irada is derived from hesitation (taraddud) and it is request (talab), while al-mashi'a is an expression for bringing into existence (ijad); so it is as if he said: 'I brought your divorce into existence,' and by it divorce occurs. Thus they mentioned. Al-Qunawi said: There is a consideration (nazar) in this; for if it were so, there would be no need for intention (niyya). The conclusion is that al-mashi'a is an expression for the complete al-irada from which the action is not withheld, and al-irada is applied to the complete and the incomplete; the first is intended with respect to Allah, exalted, and the second with respect to servants. End. Second: Al-Luqani said: The doctrine of the people of truth is that everything that Allah wills must come to be (ka'in) and everything...
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage presents two distinct cautions (tanbihan) regarding divine will. The first caution addresses the relationship between irada (will/intention) and mashi'a (wish/volition). According to the Ahl al-Sunna, these two terms are synonymous when applied to Allah; they denote the same eternal attribute. However, in human usage they are distinct. To illustrate: if a man says to his wife 'I intended (aradtu) your divorce,' the divorce does not occur (because mere intention without execution does not effect divorce), but if he says 'I wished (shi'tu) your divorce,' it does occur. The reason given is that irada derives from taraddud (hesitation) and means 'request' (talab), whereas mashi'a is an expression for 'bringing into existence' (ijad). Thus saying 'I wished your divorce' is as if saying 'I brought your divorce into existence,' which effects divorce. However, al-Qunawi critiques this explanation, noting that if mashi'a simply meant 'bringing into existence' then intention (niyya) would be unnecessary for acts. He reconciles by stating that mashi'a refers to complete irada that is inevitably followed by action, while irada can be either complete or incomplete. The complete irada is attributed to Allah (whose will is always effective), while incomplete irada occurs in servants (who may intend but not act). The second caution, though cut off, states the foundational Ahl al-Sunna principle: everything that Allah wills must come to be (ka'in), and everything... (presumably 'everything He does not will does not come to be'). This affirms that Allah's will is absolutely effective and nothing occurs outside His will.

Key Terms
الإرادة
المشيئة
التردد
الطلب
الإيجاد
الإرادة التامة
القونوي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some people think that irada and mashi'a are always interchangeable in all contexts.
Correction: In Allah they are synonymous, but in human usage they have distinct meanings: irada can be mere intention without action, while mashi'a indicates effective will that actualizes the act.

Checkpoint
According to the first tanbih, why does saying 'I wished (shi'tu) your divorce' effect divorce while 'I intended (aradtu) your divorce' does not?
A) Because mashi'a is stronger than irada
B) Because irada derives from hesitation and is mere request, while mashi'a implies bringing into existence
C) Because the word mashi'a is used in the Quran
D) Because intention requires public declaration

Key Takeaway
Irada and mashi'a are one in Allah but distinct in humans: mashi'a is complete irada that necessitates action.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say you intend to stop a sin but keep committing it, is your irada complete or incomplete? What does that reveal about the state of your heart before Allah?
?
You have the mashi'a (effective will) for worldly goals—why is your mashi'a for the Hereafter often weaker? What would it take for your will for obedience to become as effective as your will for dunya?
?
This lesson teaches that Allah's irada and mashi'a are one—always effective. How does the gap between your intentions and actions make you feel about your own sincerity? What one step can you take today to close that gap?

Definition of the Divine Will: Beyond Knowledge
Al-Maydani presents the philosophers' definition of will and the correct Ash'ari position.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari definition of divine will as an eternal attribute of specification distinct from knowledge.

Core Idea
Will is not merely knowledge; it is the attribute that specifies which possibilities become actual.

This passage from al-Maydani contrasts the philosophers' view that divine will is knowledge of the best system with the Ash'ari position that will is a distinct attribute whose function is specification. Understanding this distinction is key to grasping how Ash'ari theology accounts for the contingency and particularity of creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the two definitions
Read the Arabic passage and its translation, paying attention to the two definitions: the philosophers' view and the correct Ash'ari view as stated by al-Sa'd.
The first word 'المصلحة' is likely a corruption; the intended term is probably 'الإرادة' (will).
2
Identify the function of will
Focus on the term 'تخصيص' (specification). Why is specification necessary? Because the Essence alone relates equally to all possibilities; something must tip the scale.
3
Reflect on the argument
Consider why will cannot be reduced to knowledge. The passage argues that specification requires a distinct attribute, otherwise the Necessary Being would depend on something external—impossible.
This connects to the Ash'ari emphasis on God's independence (al-ghina).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المصلحة، وعند الحكماء والفلاسفة: هي العلم بالنظام الأكمل. والحق عندنا - كما قاله السعد: إنها صفة شأنها التخصيص قديمة زائدة قائمة به على ما هو شأن سائر الصفات الحقيقية؛ لأن تخصيص بعض الأضداد بالوقوع دون البعض وفي بعض الأوقات دون البعض مع استواء نسبة الذات إلى الكل لابد أن يكون لصفة شأنها التخصيص؛ لامتناع التخصيص بلا مخصص وامتناع احتياج الواجب في فاعليته إلى أمر منفصل. انتهى.
The [will?] and according to the wise and philosophers: it is knowledge of the most perfect system. And the truth with us - as al-Sa'd said: it is an attribute whose function is specification, eternal, superadded, subsisting in Him, like the rest of the real attributes; because specification of some opposites to occur rather than others and in some times rather than others, while the relation of the Essence to all is equal, must be [due] to an attribute whose function is specification; due to the impossibility of specification without a specifier and the impossibility of the Necessary [Being] needing in His efficacy something separate. End.
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage defines the attribute of divine will (irada). The author first mentions the definition of the philosophers and sages: they define it as knowledge of the best possible system (al-nizam al-akmal). Then he states the correct position (al-haqq) according to the Ash'ari school, quoting al-Sa'd al-Taftazani. The will is an eternal attribute (qadima) superadded to the essence (za'ida qa'ima bihi), meaning it is not identical to the essence nor a mere relation. Its function (sha'n) is specification (takhsis): it determines which of the equally possible opposites becomes actual. This specification is necessary because the Essence alone relates equally to all possibilities; without a specifying attribute, there would be no reason for one particular thing to occur. The author argues that specification cannot happen without a specifier (mukhasis), and the Necessary Being cannot depend on something external to Him for His effectivity (fa'iliyya). Therefore, the will is an essential attribute that accounts for the contingency of creation.

Key Terms
إرادة
صفة التخصيص
قديم
زائد على الذات
تخصيص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Will is merely knowledge of what is best.
Correction: Will is a distinct attribute of specification; knowledge alone does not cause one possibility to be actualized over another.

Checkpoint
What is the function (sha'n) of the attribute of will according to al-Sa'd?
A) Knowledge of the best system
B) Specification of opposites
C) Creating the universe
D) None of the above

Key Takeaway
The divine will is an eternal attribute of specification, distinct from knowledge, that determines which possibilities become actual.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
1. How does the truth that Allah's will is a distinct attribute of specification—not merely knowledge—change the way you view the specific events of your life? Can you single out one event you previously attributed to chance or general decree and now affirm that Allah willed it specifically for you? What action can you take today to embrace that specificity?
?
2. If you believed the philosophers' definition that God's will is just knowledge of the best system, how would that affect your trust that He is personally involved in your daily affairs? What does the Ash'ari insistence on an eternal specifying attribute mean for your confidence that He not only knows but actively chooses your particular circumstances?
?
3. Reflect on a moment when you struggled to accept Allah's decree over your life. Does this lesson's emphasis on specification (takhsis) remind you that no detail is too small for His will? In what area of your life do you need to submit more fully to His specific choices for you, and what concrete step will you take to surrender that area to Him?

Distinction Between Divine Will and Desire in God and Humans
Al-Maydani explains the initial distinction between irada and mashi'a and its legal application.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the terms irada and mashi'a are used differently for God and for humans according to the early scholars.

Core Idea
In God, irada and mashi'a are the same; in humans, they have different legal consequences.

This passage from al-Maydani's commentary on al-Tahawiyya introduces a key theological and legal nuance: although divine attributes may be synonymous in reference to God, their human counterparts carry distinct meanings. The discussion uses a practical example from divorce law to illustrate the difference between a mere intention (irada) and an actual bringing into existence (mashi'a).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Note the claim that for God the terms are one, but for humans they differ.
Pay attention to the legal example that follows.
2
Examine the legal example
Understand why saying 'I intended your divorce' does not effect divorce, but 'I wished your divorce' does.
Think about the difference in meaning between 'intention' and 'wish' in everyday language.
3
Reflect on the reasoning
Al-Maydani explains that irada implies hesitation and request, while mashi'a implies bringing into existence.
This linguistic etymology is key to the legal distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تنبیهان الأول: الإرادة والمشيئة واحدة عندنا في حق الله تعالى؛ أما في جانب العباد فيفترقان؛ حتى لو قال لامرأته : أردت طلاقك لا تطلق، ولو قال شئت طلاقك يقع؛ لأن الإرادة مشتقة من التردد وهو الطلب والمشيئة عبارة عن الإيجاد فكأنه قال: أوجدت طلاقك، وبه يقع الطلاق، كذا ذكروه. قال القونوي: وفيه نظر؛ إذ لو كان كذلك لما احتيج إلى النية، والحاصل أن المشيئة عبارة عن الإرادة التامة التي لا يتخلف عنها الفعل، والإرادة تطلق على التامة وعلى غير التامة؛ فالأولى هي المرادة في جانب الله تعالى، والثانية في جانب العباد. اهـ.
Two cautions: First: Irada and mashi'a are one with us regarding Allah most high; but with respect to servants they differ; such that if a man says to his wife 'I intended your divorce' she is not divorced, and if he says 'I wished your divorce' it occurs; because irada is derived from hesitation and it is request, and mashi'a is expression of bringing into existence, so it is as if he said: I have brought your divorce into existence, and by it divorce occurs. This is what they mentioned. Al-Qunawi said: There is consideration in this; for if it were so, there would be no need for intention. The conclusion is that mashi'a is the perfect irada from which action does not lag, and irada is applied to both perfect and imperfect; the first is intended with respect to Allah, and the second with respect to servants. End.
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage is a caution (tanbih) explaining the difference between the terms irada (will) and mashi'a (desire/wish) as used in Islamic theology and law. According to the author, in relation to God both terms are synonymous, but in relation to human beings they are distinct. He gives a legal example: if a man says to his wife 'I intended your divorce' (aradtu talaqak), the divorce does not take effect because irada in humans implies merely a wish or intention without immediate effect. But if he says 'I wished your divorce' (shi'tu talaqak), divorce occurs because mashi'a, in this context, is interpreted as actually bringing the divorce into existence (ijad). The author then quotes al-Qunawi, who objects that this distinction would undermine the need for explicit intention (niyya) in divorce. Al-Qunawi's resolution is that mashi'a is irada in its perfect form, which inevitably results in action, while irada is used for both perfect (irresistible) and imperfect (mere wish) forms. The perfect type applies to God, the imperfect to humans.

Key Terms
إرادة
مشيئة
تردد
طلب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think irada and mashi'a are always interchangeable in all contexts.
Correction: The passage shows they are synonymous for God but distinct in human legal contexts.

Checkpoint
What happens when a man says to his wife 'aradtu talaqak' (I intended your divorce) according to the passage?
A) She is divorced.
B) She is not divorced.
C) It depends on intention.
D) The divorce is revocable.

Key Takeaway
In God, irada and mashi'a are identical; in humans, irada indicates intention, while mashi'a indicates actualization.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How often do you use the word 'intend' about your obedience to Allah, but never actually bring it into existence? What would it look like for you today to treat a single good intention as something you have 'brought into existence'—and what is stopping you?
?
When you say 'I wish I could stop sinning,' are you leaving it as a passive wish, or are you actively bringing about the change? This lesson shows that in humans, 'wishing' can mean actualization. If you truly wished to stop, what concrete action would you take right now?
?
You have made promises to Allah—perhaps in du'a or in your heart—that you have not fulfilled. Are those promises mere intentions or actual commitments? If Allah holds you accountable for your words as if they were deeds, what must you do about the gap between your 'irada' and your 'mashi'a' before you stand before Him?

Al-Qunawi's Refinement: Mashi'a as Perfect Irada
Al-Qunawi critiques the initial distinction and offers a refined understanding.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Qunawi's objection to the simple distinction between irada and mashi'a and his resolution that mashi'a is perfect irada.

Core Idea
Mashi'a is irada in its perfect form that inevitably brings about action.

After presenting the initial distinction, al-Maydani quotes al-Qunawi, who raises a consideration. If irada and mashi'a were completely distinct as described, then the requirement of intention (niyya) in legal acts would be superfluous. Al-Qunawi resolves the issue by proposing a more nuanced understanding: mashi'a is the perfect form of irada, the one that is always followed by the intended action, applicable to God, while imperfect irada applies to humans.

Guided Path
1
Read al-Qunawi's objection
Notice his statement: 'if it were so, there would be no need for intention.'
This highlights a logical problem with the initial explanation.
2
Understand the problem
If mere mashi'a suffices to effect divorce, why is explicit intention (niyya) needed?
Think about the role of intention in legal acts.
3
Grasp his resolution
Al-Qunawi defines mashi'a as the perfect irada that necessarily produces action, while irada is a broader term.
This reconciles the initial distinction with the need for intention.
4
Apply to theology
Perfect irada (mashi'a) applies to God; imperfect irada applies to humans.
This preserves the theological synonymy while explaining human differences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تنبیهان الأول: الإرادة والمشيئة واحدة عندنا في حق الله تعالى؛ أما في جانب العباد فيفترقان؛ حتى لو قال لامرأته : أردت طلاقك لا تطلق، ولو قال شئت طلاقك يقع؛ لأن الإرادة مشتقة من التردد وهو الطلب والمشيئة عبارة عن الإيجاد فكأنه قال: أوجدت طلاقك، وبه يقع الطلاق، كذا ذكروه. قال القونوي: وفيه نظر؛ إذ لو كان كذلك لما احتيج إلى النية، والحاصل أن المشيئة عبارة عن الإرادة التامة التي لا يتخلف عنها الفعل، والإرادة تطلق على التامة وعلى غير التامة؛ فالأولى هي المرادة في جانب الله تعالى، والثانية في جانب العباد. اهـ.
Two cautions: First: Irada and mashi'a are one with us regarding Allah most high; but with respect to servants they differ; such that if a man says to his wife 'I intended your divorce' she is not divorced, and if he says 'I wished your divorce' it occurs; because irada is derived from hesitation and it is request, and mashi'a is expression of bringing into existence, so it is as if he said: I have brought your divorce into existence, and by it divorce occurs. This is what they mentioned. Al-Qunawi said: There is consideration in this; for if it were so, there would be no need for intention. The conclusion is that mashi'a is the perfect irada from which action does not lag, and irada is applied to both perfect and imperfect; the first is intended with respect to Allah, and the second with respect to servants. End.
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage is a caution (tanbih) explaining the difference between the terms irada (will) and mashi'a (desire/wish) as used in Islamic theology and law. According to the author, in relation to God both terms are synonymous, but in relation to human beings they are distinct. He gives a legal example: if a man says to his wife 'I intended your divorce' (aradtu talaqak), the divorce does not take effect because irada in humans implies merely a wish or intention without immediate effect. But if he says 'I wished your divorce' (shi'tu talaqak), divorce occurs because mashi'a, in this context, is interpreted as actually bringing the divorce into existence (ijad). The author then quotes al-Qunawi, who objects that this distinction would undermine the need for explicit intention (niyya) in divorce. Al-Qunawi's resolution is that mashi'a is irada in its perfect form, which inevitably results in action, while irada is used for both perfect (irresistible) and imperfect (mere wish) forms. The perfect type applies to God, the imperfect to humans.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the initial distinction is the complete picture without considering the need for intention.
Correction: Al-Qunawi shows that the initial view is insufficient and offers a more refined definition.

Checkpoint
According to al-Qunawi, what is mashi'a?
A) It is a type of irada that may or may not be followed by action.
B) It is the perfect irada that always results in action.
C) It is completely separate from irada.
D) It is weaker than irada.

Key Takeaway
Mashi'a is irada in its most perfect form, which always brings about its object; this perfect form belongs to God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you intend to do something for Allah but do not follow through, what does that reveal about the nature of your irada? How can you transform your imperfect irada into a mashi'a-like resolve that inevitably leads to action?
?
If Allah's mashi'a for your guidance is always realized, why do you still find yourself hesitating to obey? What does your hesitation say about the state of your own irada, and what step will you take today to align it more closely with perfect submission?
?
Al-Qunawi highlights that intention (niyya) is necessary for human actions but not for Allah's mashi'a. In what area of your life are you relying on your own intention without the perfect irada that comes from sincere faith? What would change if your irada became decisive and immediately acted upon like mashi'a?

The Absolute Efficacy of the Divine Will
Al-Maydani quotes Al-Luqani on divine will and refutes Mu'tazila.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni doctrine that everything willed by Allah necessarily occurs, and that His will encompasses both good and evil without implying His pleasure or command.

Core Idea
Allah's will is always fulfilled, even if He is not pleased with what He wills.

Al-Maydani, quoting Al-Luqani, presents the foundational Sunni belief about the divine will. This doctrine distinguishes between what Allah wills, what He loves, and what He commands. While everything that happens is willed by Allah, not everything is pleasing to Him or commanded by Him. This short passage also highlights the contrast with the Mu'tazila, who held that Allah cannot will evil.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the three key terms: مراد (willed), مرضي (pleasing), مأمور به (commanded).
The passage is dense but precise.
2
Understand the core doctrine
Internalize the statement: everything Allah wills exists, and everything that exists is willed by Him. This is the Sunni position.
This does not mean Allah is pleased with evil.
3
Distinguish will from pleasure and command
Note that things can be willed without being pleasing or commanded. This saves God from being associated with evil while affirming His absolute sovereignty.
The Salaf phrase 'ma sha Allahu kana' captures this.
4
Recognize the Mu'tazila opposition
The Mu'tazila denied that Allah wills evil, thus separating will from existence. Contrast their view with the Sunni consensus.
They opposed in two principles: that God wills evil and that His will is universal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني: قال اللقاني: مذهب أهل الحق أن كل ما أراده الله تعالى فهو كائن وكل كائن فهو مراد له تعالى وإن لم يكن مرضيا له ولا مأمورا به، وهذا ما اشتهر عن السلف ما شاء الله كان وما لم يشأ لم يكن، وخالفت المعتزلة في الأصلين. اهـ.
Second: Al-Luqani said: The doctrine of the people of truth is that everything that Allah most high wills, it exists, and everything that exists, it is willed by Him, even if it is not pleasing to Him nor commanded by Him. This is what is well-known from the Salaf: 'What Allah wills is, and what He does not will is not.' And the Mu'tazila opposed this in two principles.
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage is the second caution (tanbih) in which the author quotes Al-Luqani to affirm a core Sunni doctrine: the absolute efficacy of the divine will. The doctrine states that whatever God wills necessarily comes into existence, and conversely, anything that exists (whether good or evil, obedience or sin) is willed by God. This does not mean that God is pleased with or commands evil; rather, His will covers all occurrences, while His pleasure (rida) and command (amr) are restricted to good. The author notes that this was the position of the Salaf (early Muslims) encapsulated in the phrase 'ma sha'a Allahu kana wa ma lam yasha' lam yakun' (What Allah wills is, and what He does not will is not). The Mu'tazilites opposed this on two principles: they denied that God wills evil acts and argued that God must will what He commands, thus separating will from command.

Key Terms
مذهب أهل الحق
مراد
مرضي
مأمور به

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God wills sin, He must be pleased with it or command it.
Correction: The passage clarifies that will (iraada) is distinct from pleasure (rida) and command (amr). God can will something without being pleased with it or commanding it.

Checkpoint
What is the meaning of the Salaf's statement 'ما شاء الله كان وما لم يشأ لم يكن'?
A) Only good exists
B) Everything is willed by God
C) God does not will evil
D) Human free will determines events

Key Takeaway
Allah's will is absolute: whatever He wills occurs, even if He is not pleased with it or does not command it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Reflect on a moment when you experienced something painful or sinful. Do you truly believe that Allah willed that event to happen, even though He is not pleased with it? How does this change your understanding of His sovereignty and your own responsibility? Commit to accepting His will fully, even when you do not understand.
?
Is there an area of your life where you act as if something happened outside of Allah's will? For example, do you ever blame chance or people while forgetting that nothing occurs except by His will? Write down one specific situation and consciously affirm: 'Allah willed this, and it is exactly what He wanted to happen.' How does that feel?
?
The Salaf said, 'What Allah wills is, and what He does not will is not.' Does your heart truly rest in this? Or do you secretly wish that some things had not been willed by Allah? Bring one such thing to mind and pray for the strength to accept His decree while still striving to obey His commands.

Divine Attributes

The Divine Attribute of Life
Definition and explanation of God's attribute of life as eternal and condition for other attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that God's attribute of life is eternal, subsisting in His essence, and a rational condition for all other attributes.

Core Idea
Life is an eternal attribute that makes God's other attributes possible.

The first phrase of the Tahawiyyah Creed declares that Allah is Living and does not die. In this lesson, we examine what 'Living' means as a divine attribute: an eternal quality inherent in God's essence, which is a necessary condition for His other attributes such as knowledge and power.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of Hayy
Read the passage where 'Living' is defined as being described with the attribute of life, an eternal attribute subsisting in His essence.
Notice that life is described as not relating to anything external.
2
Understand life as an eternal attribute
The passage states that life is eternal (azaliyya) and everlasting (abadiyya). Reflect on what it means for an attribute to be eternal.
Eternal here means without beginning or end.
3
Grasp life as a rational condition
Life is a condition (shart ʻaqli) for other attributes. Ask yourself: why must God be living for Him to have knowledge or power?
The condition is logical, not temporal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حَيٌّ لَا يَمُوتُ.
شرح العقيدة الطحاوية المسماة: بيان السنة والجماعة.
[الحياة)
(حي) أي موصوف بصفة الحياة، وهي صفة أزلية قائمة بذاته تعالى لا تتعلق بشيء، وهي شرط عقلي لسائر الصفات، كما أن الوجود شرط لها. واعلم أن المصنف قد أعرض عن بحث الوجود واكتفي بما هو ظاهر في مقام الشهود؛ ففي التنزيل: (قَالَتْ رُسُلُهُمْ أَفِي اللَّهِ شَكٍّ فَاطِرِ السَّمَوَاتِ وَالْأَرْضِ الآية (سورة إبراهيم آية: ١٠]، وَلَبِن سَأَلْتَهُم مِّنْ خَلَقَ السَّمَوَاتِ وَالأَرْضَ لَيَقُولُنَّ الله) [سورة لقمان آية: (۲٥)؛ فوجود الحق ثابت في فطرة الخلق كما يشير إليه قوله تعالى: فِطْرَتَ اللهِ الَّتِي فَطَرَ النَّاسَ عَلَيْهَا) [سورة الروم آبة: ٣٠)، ويومى إليه حديث: «كُلُّ مَوْلُودٍ عَلَى الفِطْرَةِ » (١) ، وإنما جاء الأنبياء عليهم السلام لبيان التوحيد وتبيان التفريد؛ ولذا أطبقت كلمتهم واجتمعت حجتهم على كلمة التوحيد بأن يقولوا: لا إله إلا الله، ولم يأمروا أهل ملتهم بأن يقولوا: الله موجود، بل قصدوا إظهار أن غيره ليس بمعبود رداً لما توهموا وتخيلوا حيث قالوا: هَؤُلَاءِ شُفَعَدُونَا عِندَ اللهِ) [سورة يونس آية: ١٨]، ﴿ مَا تَعْبُدُهُمْ إِلَّا لِيُقَرِّبُونَا إِلَى الله زُلفى ) [سورة الزمر آية: ٣]، على أن التوحيد يفيد الوجود مع مزيد التأييد؛ ولذا صدر عقيدته بشهادة التوحيد. وكما أن حياته أزلية فهي أبدية كما نص عليها بقوله: (لا يموت) أي أبدا؛ إذ من ثبت قدمه استحال عدمه.
(۱) متفق عليه؛ أخرجه البخاري في كتاب الجنائز، باب ما قيل في أولاد المشركين حديث رقم (١٣٨٥)، ومسلم في كتاب القدر، باب معنى كل مولود يولد على الفطرة، حديث رقم
(٢٦٥٨)، عن أبي هريرة رضي الله عنه.
He is Living, does not die. [Commentary on al-ʻAqida al-Tahawiyya named: Bayan al-Sunna wa al-Jamaʻa. [Life]] (Living) i.e. described with the attribute of life, which is an eternal attribute subsisting in His Essence, the Exalted, not relating to anything, and it is a rational condition for the rest of the attributes, just as existence is a condition for it. And know that the author turned away from discussing existence and sufficed with what is apparent in the station of witnessing; for in the Revelation: (Their messengers said: Is there any doubt about Allah, the Originator of the heavens and the earth? verse [Surah Ibrahim: 10], And if you ask them who created the heavens and the earth, they will surely say: Allah [Surah Luqman: 25]; so the existence of the Truth is established in the innate disposition (fitra) of creation, as indicated by His saying: (the fitra of Allah upon which He created mankind) [Surah al-Rum: 30], and it is alluded to by the hadith: 'Every child is born upon the fitra' (1). And the prophets, peace be upon them, only came to clarify tawhid and to explain the uniqueness of Allah; therefore their word was unanimous and their argument agreed upon the word of tawhid, that they say: There is no god but Allah. They did not command the people of their religion to say: Allah exists, but rather they intended to show that other than Him is not worshipped, in refutation of what they fancied and imagined when they said: (These are our intercessors with Allah) [Surah Yunus: 18], (We do not worship them except to bring us nearer to Allah) [Surah al-Zumar: 3]. Moreover, tawhid implies existence with additional emphasis; therefore he began his creed with the testimony of tawhid. And just as His life is eternal, so it is everlasting, as he stated by his saying: (does not die) meaning forever; for whosoever's eternity is established, his non-existence is impossible. (1) Agreed upon; narrated by Bukhari in the Book of Funerals, Chapter on what is said about the children of polytheists, hadith no. (1385), and Muslim in the Book of Destiny, Chapter on the meaning of every child being born upon the fitra, hadith no. (2658), on the authority of Abu Hurayra, may Allah be pleased with him.
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the phrase 'حَيٌّ لَا يَمُوتُ' (He is Living, does not die) from the Tahawiyyah creed. The author defines 'Living' (Hayy) as being described with the attribute of life, which is eternal, subsisting in God's essence, and not relating to anything external. Life is a rational condition (shart ʻaqli) for other attributes, just as existence is a condition for life itself. The author then explains why the creed's author (al-Tahawi) did not explicitly discuss God's existence: because it is self-evident (zahir) in the station of witnessing (maqam al-shuhud). Evidence is drawn from Quranic verses (14:10, 31:25, 30:30) and the hadith affirming that every child is born upon the fitra. The prophets' mission was not to prove existence but to teach tawhid, because people already acknowledged God's existence but associated partners with Him. The passage concludes by affirming the eternity and everlastingness of God's life: 'does not die' means His non-existence is impossible once His pre-eternity is established. This lesson combines theological definition (life) with a proof for God's existence based on innate human disposition.

أزلية
شرط عقلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think God's life is similar to human life, which is temporary and dependent on the body.
Correction: God's life is an eternal attribute subsisting in His essence, not a bodily life; it does not relate to anything and is a condition for other attributes.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does it mean that life is a 'rational condition' (شرط عقلي) for the other attributes?
A) Life is necessary for the attributes to be rationally conceivable.
B) Life is a prerequisite for the existence of other attributes in God.
C) Life is a condition that reason imposes on the attributes.
D) Life is the most important attribute.

Key Takeaway
God's life is an eternal, self-subsisting attribute that is a necessary condition for all His other attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's life is eternal and He never dies, what does that mean for the fleeting anxieties you cling to? Are you living as if the One who knows all is truly alive and aware of your every thought? Commit to one area where you will act today on the certainty that God sees and knows.
?
The attribute of life is the condition for all other attributes. How does this truth confront your moments of doubt when you feel that God is distant or unaware? Write down one situation where you doubted God's awareness and counter it with the declaration that His life is eternal and ever-present.
?
God's life does not relate to anything outside His essence. How does this free you from the idea that God needs your worship or obedience to be 'complete'? What would change in your worship if you truly believed God is perfectly self-sufficient and alive without needing anything from you?
